==PAGE:1==
 
 
 
 
 
 Environmental Sustainability of Support  
Fuels in Central Power Plants 
Use of Life Cycle Assessment as decision support in DONG Energy  
Mette Frigg Skindhøj Rames, s093159 
Master thesis at the Technical University of Denmark, Department of 
Environmental Engineering and Management Engineering 
Supervisors: Assistant Professor Alexis Laurent and  
Associate Professor Niki Bey, DTU Management Engineering 
==PAGE:2==
Abstract 
The world’s energy supply is currently based largely on fossil energy sources, which are considered limited 
resources and known to cause severe environmental impacts. Despite the focus on changing the primary fuels to 
bio-based alternatives, especially wood pellets, support fuels (SFs) are still needed at dust-fired power plants 
when running on low loads such as during shut-down and start-up. This study provides information of 
environmental performance of fuels suited as SFs at dust-fired power plants specifically. The relevant fuels were 
first identified by creating a broad overview of biofuels and then shortlisting the SF options by identifying a list 
of criteria the fuels need to live up to, to be used as SFs. A comparative Life Cycle Assessment including 
normalisation and weighting with the ReCiPe framework was made based on data from literature and Ecoinvent 
3.0 database for the current SFs, Heavy Fuel Oil (HFO) and Natural Gas (NG), and the seven bio-based HFO 
alternatives order of increasing impact: Jatropha oil, Jatropha biodiesel, UCO biodiesel, UCO (Used Cooking 
Oil), algal biodiesel, algal oil, pyrolysis oil, and lastly biogas as the alternative to natural gas. All the alternative 
SFs had lower environmental impacts than the fossils, both at midpoint and endpoint level, and of at least a 
factor four for the weighted endpoint scores. The impact was also lower for the bio-based SFs for sensitivity 
scenarios, confirming that they provide environmental improvement potential. For the algal fuels the conclusion 
is not as definitive due to the wide variations in inventory data found in literature, because the technology is still 
new and many variations of it still exists. It was noted that especially the climate change and fossil depletion 
impacts were influencing the scores. Regarding the various power plants it was found that the environmental 
performance was directly correlated to the production of useful energy at low loads.  
 
==PAGE:3==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
i 
 
Resume 
Størstedelen af verdens energiproduktion er i dag baseret på fossile brændsler, der opfattes som begrænsede 
ressourcer og som har negativ indvirkning på miljøet. På trods af det fokus der er på at udskifte primærbrændsler 
på kraftværker til bio-baserede brændsler, fx træpiller, har værkerne stadig brug for støttebrændsler ved lavlast 
drift, fx under opstart og nedlukning af kedler. Denne rapport omhandler specifikt den miljømæssige 
bæredygtighed af alternative støttebrændsler på støvfyrede kraftværker. De mulige alternativer blev fundet ved at 
sortere i en lang liste af biobrændsler beskrevet i litteraturen ved hjælp af en række kriterier opstillet specifikt til 
formålet, som skal opfyldes for at brændslerne er anvendelige som støttebrændsler. En komparativ livscyklus 
vurdering (LCA), inklusiv normalisering og vægtning med ReCiPe metoden, blev udført på baggrund af data fra 
litteraturen og Ecoinvent 3.0 databasen for de nuværende støttebrændsler: Heavy Fuel Oil (HFO) og Naturgas 
samt de syv oliealternativer (i rækkefølge efter stigende påvirkning): Jatropha oile, Jatropha biodiesel, UCO 
biodiesel, UCO (Used Cooking Oil), alge-biodiesel, algeoile, pyrolyseoile. Og derudover biogas som alternativ 
til naturgas. Alle de biobaserede støttebrændsler viste lavere miljøpåvirkning end de fossile, både for 
midtpunkts- og slutpunkts analyserne, og med mindst en faktor fire for de vægtede slutpunkts enkeltværdier. 
Miljøpåvirkningen var også lavere for følsomhedsscenarierne for de biobaserede støttebrændsler, hvilket 
underbygger at de udgør et miljøforbedringspotentiale. For de algebaserede brændsler er konklusionen ikke så 
definitiv som for de øvrige på grund af de store variationer i data fundet i litteraturen, der skyldes at det stadig er 
en teknologi der bevæger sig i mange retninger. Det blev bemærket at især klimaændringer og brugen af fossile 
ressourcer havde stor indflydelse på resultaterne. Desuden var miljøpåvirkningen for de forskellige kraftværker 
direkte afhængig af produktionen af brugbar energi ved lavlast mens støttebrændslerne bruges.

Preface 
This master thesis in Environmental Technology was carried out at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, 
from January 2nd 2015 to June 2nd 2015. I would like to thank my main supervisor for the project Adjunct at 
DTU Management Engineering Alexis Laurent for his support, criticism and suggestions, and also I would like 
to thank my co-supervisor Niki Bey, Lector at DTU Management Engineering.  
From DONG Energy I would like to thank the company representative for the project, Director Jens Price Wolf, 
for the opportunity to collaborate on this project. Special thanks go to the people in DONG Energy who helped 
me with information and data for my project; Benny Göbel, Ole Staunsgaard and Per Ottosen for introduction to 
power plant technique and the use support fuels; Søren Hørlyck, Rikke A. Hansen, Victoria Menqi Yang, Jan 
Hvidberg and Hans-Henrik Friis-Pedersen for providing data about fuel use and support fuels at the different 
power plants; Peter Kofod Kristensen for information about sustainability policies in DONG Energy.  
The thesis is based on the structure of a scientific article that synthesises the work and extract the key messages, 
and is therefore limited in length. Detailed descriptions and data can be found in appendices A-G. In the methods 
chapter the possible alternative support fuels are identified based on a set of criteria developed specifically for 
the purpose. The chapter also explains how these criteria were developed based on the requirements fuels have to 
live up to, to be used at DONG Energy's plants. A description is also given of how the LCA (Life Cycle 
Assessment) principles of the ILCD Handbook and ISO 14000 series are going to be used when conducting the 
environmental assessment of the identified alternative support fuels. The results of the LCA modelling are 
presented in the results and discussion chapter for each of the studied fuels, including sensitivity assessments of 
the most vital assumptions.  
 
 
 
 
2nd of June 2014            Mette Rames, s093159

Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
1 
 
1. Introduction 
The worldwide primary energy supply relies heavily on fossil fuels with more than 80% came from the fossil 
sources coal, oil, and natural gas in 2012 (IEA, 2014).  The fossil fuels are known to cause significant impacts to 
the environment of which climate change is regarded one of the most important. Also the fossil fuels are limited 
resources and energy security is therefore another reason to look into alternative, renewable energy sources. Due 
to these fossil fuel issues political consensus of reducing greenhouse gas emissions has been achieved and 
incentives towards using renewable, low-carbon energy have emerged. In EU this has resulted in the 2020 goal 
of 20% renewables in the primary energy supply by 2020 and 27% by 2030 (European Commission, 2014) and 
in Denmark the fossils should be phased out completely by 2050 (The Danish Government, 2013).  
 
Even though large power producers such as Drax and E-On have started to use biomass, primarily wood pellets, 
for power production (SBP, 2015), the biomass only replaces the primary fuel at the plants. Wood pellets are, 
like coal, grinded to fine dust and blown into the power plant boiler with high pressure air, however, the boiler 
cannot run on low loads on coal/wood pellet dust, and therefore a support fuel (SF) is needed at low loads such 
as start-up and shut-down.  The SF is usually natural gas or oil, since these fuels can be fired in without air and 
hence has no risk of back-burning at low firing rates. When using wood pellets instead of coal at power plants it 
is thus only the primary fuel, the coal, that is replaced, but not the support fuel, which will still be fossil oil or 
gas. The amount of SF needed differs according to multiple factors, including the need for running at low loads, 
number of start-ups, and how flexible the boiler is, i.e. how low loads can be reached using the primary fuel 
only. For base load power plants the number of start-ups is usually less than 15 per year, whereas power plants 
operated at two-shift (off load at night and on load at day) can have up to 200 start-ups per year, which is 
increasingly common for most dust fired power plants  (DECC, 2014). The amount of annual fuel input made up 
by SFs can thus vary a lot, from below 1% to several percent. Regardless of the share of SFs their environmental 
impacts will be increasingly significant as the primary fuels are changed from fossil to bio-based, while the SFs 
are still fossils. Despite efforts to optimize the SF use (DECC, 2014; DONG Energy) it is therefore important for 
the overall environmental performance of dust fired power plants to consider renewable alternatives as SFs.  
 
To assess the environmental performance of different fuels and energy sources in a holistic manner, the life cycle 
assessment (LCA) is widely used. LCA is an ISO-standardized decision-support tool that is used to quantify the 
environmental impacts from a product or a system in a life cycle perspective. Even though the use of SFs is a 
widespread technology for pulverized coal power plants (Kokopeli et al, 2013), only very little scientific 
literature exist on the subject, and no LCA’s of SFs has been published to the author’s knowledge. Most LCA’s 
comparing fossil oil and natural gas with bio-based alternatives regard the use in direct injection engines and 
usually the studies concern only the assessment of a single fuel.  
 
This study aims to provide information of environmental performance of fuels suited as SFs at dust-fired power 
plants specifically. In order to make such a comparison the relevant fuels were first identified by creating a broad 
overview of biofuels and then shortlisting the SF options by identifying a list of criteria the fuels need to live up 
==PAGE:8==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
2 
 
to, to be used as SFs. Secondly the LCA methodology was used to (1) identify environmental hotspots and 
discuss uncertainties in the production of the fuels in a cradle-to-gate perspective to identify environmentally 
preferred feedstock and production pathways, (2) Compare the fuels at plant level including the combustion 
process (i.e. cradle-to-grave perspective) and give general recommendations of plant specifications to increase 
sustainability, (4) Compare the fuels aggregated at company level using by site-specific data from DONG 
Energy and upscaling the SF consumption to annual company-wide data to provide recommendations for SF use 
in DONG Energy.  
 
In order to assess SF use at plant and company level SF-specific data provided by DONG Energy has been used, 
since SFs are combusted at the power plants under different conditions (e.g. low loads such as during start-ups) 
than the primary fuels, and generic power plant data therefore could not be used. As the leading energy company 
in Denmark DONG Energy also work towards sustainability goals and to increase the share of renewable energy 
in their portfolio. This is achieved by increasing wind power capacity (2.5 TWh in 2104) and by converting coal 
fired central power plants to biomass, primarily wood pellets. As is the case for most wood pellet power plants, 
the focus is on changing the solid primary fuel, but there has not yet been any focus on changing the SFs. Even 
though the share of SF might be low compared  to primary fuel share, DONG Energy central power plants 
produce one third (31.4 PJ) of the district heating and one fourth (7.8 TWh) of the electricity demand in 
Denmark with around 50% of the fuels being renewables by 2020 (DONG Energy, 2014). Hence with the 
increasing conversion to renewable primary fuels, the importance of replacing fossil SFs increases. Even though 
data from DONG Energy is used the results of the study is a good indication of SF use in other countries and 
companies using dust-fired power plants in electricity and heat production.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:9==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
3 
 
2. Methods 
2.1. 
Preliminary research 
In order to address sustainability performance of SFs and identify alternatives to the current fossil SFs, a good 
understanding of power plant operations and how SFs are used at dust-fired power plants is necessary. Since the 
literature on SFs specifically is limited or only describes the subject in very general terms, interviews with 
DONG Energy employees founded the basis of the SF-description found in appendix B. In this knowledge 
gathering process the Chain Referral Sampling or “snowball” method was used to identify the employees with 
the needed knowledge to gain insight into the use of SFs and technical power plant knowledge (Biernacki and 
Waldorf, 1981). During this preliminary research a wide literature search was performed on the subject of 
biofuels to investigate the status of different biofuel technologies and get and up-to-date overview of 
possibilities. This resulted in a long list of biofuels (Appendix C) including all types of fluid or gaseous type of 
fuels that might be used as SFs.  
2.2. 
Criteria and short-listing of fuel alternatives 
Due to the limited time and resources for the study not all of the fuels on the longlist could be assessed in detail, 
and therefore a shortlisting was made to identify the most suitable bio-based SF alternatives. The shortlisting 
was based on a list of criteria including sustainability, technical-, and market-related measures that the fuels 
would have to live up to, to be viable alternatives for the fossil SFs. Since the SFs are evaluated on a technology-
basis only criteria related directly to the technologies or feedstock of the fuels are considered here. Aspects 
related to the specific supply chain or management practices that cannot be generalised for specific fuel 
technologies or feedstock types, are not used to shortlist the fuels and are therefore not included in the criteria 
list. More details about the criteria can be seen in Appendix D.  
2.2.1 Sustainability related criteria 
The sustainability criteria covers overall social and environmental considerations and is meant to ensure that the 
use of bio-based SFs does not cause burden shifting, i.e. create new problems when solving the existing ones. 
Two criteria in this category are used to sort out specific fuels and feedstock from the longlist: (1) the fuel cannot 
be first generation; (2) the fuel cannot have other direct and obvious better uses.  
The first criterion relates to the feedstock and the general consensus that first generation biofuels are not 
sustainable due to the direct competition between food and fuel that they offset (European Federation for 
Transport and Environment, 2015). First generation biofuels are here considered to be all fuels made from 
feedstock that could have been used for human food such as maize, sugar or eatable vegetable oils.   
The second criterion relates to the specific technology and hence type of fuel. In practice this criterion rules out 
drop-in fuels for the transport sector. These fuels include renewable diesel and biogasoline, which have chemical 
structures almost identical to that of fossil diesel and gasoline used for car engines, and hence can be used 
directly with current infrastructure and motor technologies, without being blended with fossil fuels. Thus the 
drop-in fuels are developed specifically and refined to a degree where they can replace fossil diesel and gasoline 
in the transport sector 1:1. The renewable diesel and biogasoline are thus not considered for use at power plants.  
==PAGE:10==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
4 
 
2.2.2. Technical criteria 
The technical criteria relate directly to the physical and chemical properties of the fuels and power plants. 
However, the exact numerical values of the upper/lower limits of these properties are not known, and therefore 
the currently used SFs (natural gas and oil) are used as indicators for what these values should be.  
An important criterion for energy companies that are profit-driven is to have a low capex (capital expenditure) 
for development projects such as replacing SFs. Therefore the new SF should be compatible with the existing 
feeding mechanism and handling systems at the power plant. For most dust-fired power plants, including those 
in DONG Energy’s portfolio, the SFs Heavy Fuel Oil (HFO) or in some cases natural gas. This criterion hence 
limits the alternative SFs to oily and gaseous fuels, which excludes for instance bio-alcohols (such as bio 
ethanol). This criterion also limits the gaseous compounds to those that can be delivered via the existing natural 
gas grid, since this is how DONG Energy receives the natural gas today, and no storage exist at the power plants 
for gaseous SFs, which is most likely also the case at other power plants.  
Even though the system compatibility criterion limits the fuels to oil and gas substances, which have the same 
physical appearance as the fossil SFs, the more specific physical properties should also fit the purpose of the 
SFs. The purpose is primarily to be used at start-up and shut-down of the boiler and in other low-load 
circumstances, and therefore the heating value, flash point and viscosity has been identified as important 
physical properties for the SFs. These properties are important for the ability of the fuels to ignite and create a 
flame of the right size and shape while providing enough heat to heat up the boiler and ignite the primary fuel. 
Since no in situ experiments have been made with alternative SFs the exact required value of these properties 
and their relative importance are not known. Therefore the best indication is to use the fuel oil and natural gas 
properties as underlying basis for what the physical properties of the alternatives should resemble. It should be 
kept in mind that other properties such as cetane number, ash content, and vapour pressure etc. of the fuels might 
also be important. Most feeding mechanisms can be adjusted to provide SF in a higher or lower rate or handle 
fuels of a different viscosity/temperature, which allows for some variance in the properties.  Due to limited 
knowledge on the area and these variation possibilities none of the SF alternatives are sorted out based on this 
criterion, and also most of the oil-based fuels heating values, flash point, and viscosity within the same range 
(Appendix D). 
Related to the physical properties of the SFs are the chemical properties. A number of compounds are known to 
cause corrosion of boilers such as chlorine and high alkali contents. Other compounds, especially ash 
compounds, might cause slagging and fouling of the boiler, which increases maintenance costs and decreases 
power plant efficiencies. The chemical contents often depends on the feedstock for the fuels and the production 
processes, but real life experiments are usually required to determine whether a fuel cause problems or not. No 
reports of such damaging compounds were discovered for the investigated fuels. Another important aspect of the 
chemistry is the content of compounds limited by legislation such as heavy metals, SO2 and NOx gasses. Heavy 
metals and SO2 are usually much lower for bio-based than fossil fuels and therefore not considered to be a risk. 
For NOx gasses articles have been found that indicate larger emissions from biofuels, however, these are based 
on experiments with direct-ignition engines (i.e. common diesel engines), which is a very different technology 
from power plant boilers, which also often have De-Nox catalysts (Macor and Pavanello, 2009; Oszesen et al. 
2009; Buyukkaya, 2010).  
==PAGE:11==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
5 
 
2.2.3. Market related criteria 
The market related criteria relate to the availability and cost of fuels. For profit-driven companies it is important 
that the alternative SFs pose a good business case and are competitive with the currently used fuels. Whether this 
is the case depends on legislative frames, subsidy and tax schemes, and on the fuel price. The first might change 
over time and from country to country, and is therefore difficult to generalise. The fuel price is difficult to 
determine as well, since most of the technologies and fuels considered (excluding the first generation biofuels) 
are still under development and some of them are not currently marketed. Since this study is not an economic 
assessment, it is therefore outside the scope to provide a detailed market assessment.   
This study takes a long-term view, and the market-related criteria are therefore not used to assess the current 
economic feasibility of each fuel, but used to identify the most promising feedstock and technologies in a longer 
perspective. Since market availability and price are strongly correlated with technology development and fuels 
will become more economically feasible as technology develops, also emerging technologies are considered in 
this study (such as 2nd and 3rd generation biofuels), even though they might not be competitive at the moment. 
Also only the most promising technologies are taken into account, as these are more likely to develop into large-
scale competitive productions in the future. For instance for biodiesel production the two-step transesterification 
method is therefore used, and for oil extraction of plant oils the hexane solvent-method is used (Sayyar et al., 
2009). Another example is that of Danish biogas, where a report from 2014 (Energisyrelsen, 2014) shows that 
the majority of biogas in Denmark is produced from manure, and only very little is produced from sewage 
sludge or landfills, and hence these two technologies are not considered, since they are technologically inferior.  
2.2.4. Short-list 
Based on the criteria for support fuels all the options on the long list (Appendix C) are shortlisted to three 
alternatives for fuel oil (crude vegetable oils, biodiesel, and pyrolysis oil) and biogas from manure gasification as 
the alternative to natural gas as seen in table 2.1. For the crude vegetable oils and biodiesel three feedstock types 
are considered: used cooking oil (UCO) from Denmark, Jatropha oil from India, and Algal oil form Denmark.  
Table 2.1: Shortlist of possible alternative support fuels in Dong Energy. See Detailed description of the shortlisting in appendix D 
2.3. 
Life Cycle assessment 
2.3.1. Assessment framework 
In order to evaluate the environmental performance of the shortlisted alternative SFs the impact assessments are 
performed at three levels; fuel level, plant level, and company level. The fuel level assessment is a cradle-to-gate 
assessment where the hotspots in the production of each fuel are assessed without comparing the fuels. 
Comparison is not possible at fuel level, since the combustion process is not included and factors such as energy 
content of the fuels therefore not considered. The plant level assessment is a cradle-to-grave assessment

6 
 
including combustion of 1 MJ of fuel, which entails that the fuels can be compared directly because they are 
evaluated based on the same service delivered (1 MJ input energy).  At the plant level the power plants can also 
be compared by assessing the impact of burning 1 MJ of the same type of fuel at each plant. At the company 
level the annual aggregated SF consumption at DONG Energy power plants is assessed to evaluate the total 
environmental effect of changing SF fuel.  
2.3.2. Scope of the life cycle assessment 
In order to be able to compare the current and alternative options for SFs at DONG Energy power plants, it is 
important that the fuels assessed provide the same service to the system. This service is defined both 
quantitatively and qualitatively by the Functional Unit (FU): 
The necessary amount of support fuel that provides an all year-round ability to 1) ignite the primary dust fuel in 
the current DE CHP plant set-up, and 2) be used as an emergency and peak load fuel on all relevant DONG 
Energy CHP plants in an average year. 
 
a. the necessary amount: [1519 TJ gas or 423 TJ oil annually] 
b. Current set-up: shall have the ability to be handled by the current feeding mechanism 
c. Relevant plants: plants using the same type of SF (i.e. either all HFO or all NG using plants) 
d. In an average year: Based on an average of 2013-2014 data (Appendix F) 
Since it is not physically possible to use oily SFs on current natural gas systems and vice versa, it is not relevant 
to compare oily and gaseous SFs to each other. The reference flows of each alternative SF are based on the 
functional unit and the energy content (calorific value) of the fuels, and can be seen in Appendix A, table A1.The 
specific system boundaries differ for each type of fuel and feedstock due to varying production pathways, but the 
general configuration is the same, as shown in figure 2.1. The feedstock and the processing steps, marked with 
orange, are different for each type of fuel and feedstock dependent on whether the feedstock is a waste product 
or cultivated for the purpose and on which processing steps are needed for the various feedstock types. The 
transesterification step, i.e. the biodiesel production from crude oils, is only valid for the biodiesel types. The 
specific system boundaries for each of the assessed SFs can be found in Appendix A, section 2.2 (figure A1-A5). 
All the processes necessary to produce the various SFs are included in the system boundaries, also infrastructure 
processes and production facilities and machinery. Due to the use of the consequential LCA framework, co-
products are included in the system boundaries through system expansion.

2.3.3. Life cycle inventories 
The life cycle inventories (LCIs) for all fuels are based on the Ecoinvent 3.0 database (Ecoinvent, 2013) and data 
found in literature through a comprehensive literature search (Appendix E), especially focusing on published 
LCA studies of the selected fuels. In case of multiple production pathways for a fuel type, the state-of-the-art and 
most developed/most widespread processes are used, because they are the ones most likely to be available on the 
market. The in- and outputs of materials and energy from the fuel production processes are combined with the 
inventories in the Ecoinvent 3.0 consequential database, which is currently the most comprehensive LCI 
database available, in order to determine the exchanges of pollutants and resource consumptions of the processes 
(Laurent and Espinosa, 2015).  For the use phase (i.e. burning the fuel in CHPs) inventory data was provided by 
DONG Energy from direct fuel use and energy production (heat and power) measurements when using SFs at 
the central power plants (Appendix G). 
Natural gas and HFO 
The fossil SFs used as baseline for comparison in the study are natural gas and Heavy Fuel Oil (HFO), which are 
currently used at DONG Energy and most similar power plants (DECC, 2014). The inventories for extraction, 
refining and transportation of natural gas and HFO at the Danish market are taken directly from the Ecoinvent 
3.0 database (Ecoinvent, 2013).  For the natural gas the inventory data in Ecoinvent is obtained from literature 
and environmental reports, and leakage data form other European countries. For the HFO the process for 
‘Europe without Switzerland’ was used and adjusted to Danish electricity input only. Both processes include all 
infrastructure processes as well.  
==PAGE:14==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
8 
 
Biogas 
The biogas is assumed to be produced in Denmark. The feedstock inputmix for the biogas production is based on 
data from Jacobsen et al. (2013) and consists of 78% liquid and 12% solid pig and cattle manure and 10% maize 
silage (Appendix A, table A.3). The maize silage is needed to increase the dry matter content and thereby the 
biogas production (Jacobsen et al., 2013).  With this inputmix 25.3 kg is needed to produce 1 m3 biogas (60% 
methane), based on Schmidt and Brandão (2013).  It is assumed that the biogas is upgraded to 96% methane and 
delivered to the power plants through the natural gas grid in Denmark. Leakage in the grid is based on natural 
gas transportation in grid from Ecoinvent 3.0.   
The upgrading is done by removing CO2 from the biogas to reach 96% methane content as described in Schmidt 
and Brandão (2013). The removed CO2 is modelled as a co-product by system expansion, since it can be sold 
and used in other industrial processes. It is assumed that the manure is transported directly from the stable to the 
biogas plant, hence avoiding the manure storage otherwise needed according to Schmidt and Brandão (2013). 
The emissions from manure storage are based on manure composition and emission factors from Poulsen et al. 
(2001), Schmidt and Brandão (2013) and Wesnæs et al. (2009).  
The Danish energy agency (Energistyrelsen, 2013) reported that the nutrients from gasified manure are easier 
utilised by the plants than from raw manure when used as field fertiliser. Lemvig Biogas (2013) reported the real 
N-exploitation on-field to be 70% for raw manure (based on the input mix) and 83% for gasified manure. This 
difference was modelled as the extra mineral N-fertiliser needed to compensate for lower N-exploitation, which 
is around 0.81 kg. It is assumed that P and K exploitation is the same for raw and gasified manure and that there 
is no significant loss of nutrients during gasification. More details can be found in Appendix A, section 3.1. 
UCO and UCO biodiesel 
The UCO (Used Cooking Oil) is assumed to originate in Denmark and since it is a waste product the production 
of the oil is not included. A collection radius of 100 km by truck is assumed. The UCO can either be used 
directly after purification (removal of water and organic particles by heating) or refined into biodiesel. The 
inventory of the purification process is based on Piero et al. (2008) and Piero et al. (2010), which are also used as 
sources for the transesterification process. The transesterification is performed with sulphuric acid (H2SO4) and 
potassium hydroxide (KOH) as catalysts and glycerine and potassium sulphate fertiliser (K2SO4) are produced as 
co-products. More details in Appendix A, section 3.2.   
Jatropha oil and biodiesel 
The Jatropha oil is assumed to be produced in India with the inventory based on numbers from Ajayebi et al. 
(2013) and Whitaker and Heath (2009) for cultivation and extraction. According to various sources the dried 
seed husk is assumed to be incinerated for electricity generation (1.03 MJ/kg oil), and the seed cake is used as 
fertiliser on the Jatropha fields with a fertiliser potential of 0.15 kg NPK (40:20:10) replacement per kg seed 
cake (Whitaker and Heath, 2009; Ajayebi et al., 2013; Kumar et al., 2012). The oil content is assumed to be 35% 
and the extraction efficiency 91% (Kumar et al., 2012). The hexane solvent extraction process is used, since it 
gives the highest efficiency (Sayyar et al., 2009).  
The transesterification process for Jatropha biodiesel is based on Whitaker and Heath (2009) and Ajayebi et al. 
(2013), which are essentially the same. The catalysts used are sodium hydroxide (NaOH) and sulphuric acid 
(H2SO4) with glycerine as the only co-product. More details in Appendix A, section 3.3.  
==PAGE:15==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
9 
 
Algal oil and biodiesel 
For the algal oil the raceway pond cultivation system was assumed, since it is a more mature technology with 
better energy balance (Stephenson et al., 2010). Due to great variation in inventories for most of the life cycle 
phases, data from Stephenson et al. (2010) is used for inventories of all the production processes. This also 
implies that the assumptions made in Stephenson et al. (2010) are also made in this study. These assumptions 
include that the freshwater algae Chlorella vulgaris is grown in a two-step cultivation process, the first under 
nitrogen-sufficient conditions to increase concentration of biomass, the second under nitrogen-limited conditions 
to increase lipid production in the algae cells. Also the culture media is assumed to be recycled and after oil 
extraction algal residue is used to produce methane by anaerobic digestion, which is then burned for energy 
recovery. Two assumptions in this study vary from those in Stephenson et al. (2010); first that the CO2 taken up 
by algae comes from the air, not flue gas from a power plant, and second the facility is modelled with an 
anaerobic digestion plant from Ecoinvent 3.0 as proxy rather than the building materials listed in Stephenson et 
al. (2010). Stephenson et al. (2010) is used as source for the transesterification process for algal biodiesel, where 
potassium hydroxide (KOH) and sulphuric acid (H2SO4) are used as catalysts and the co-products glycerine and 
potassium sulphate fertiliser (K2SO4) are produced. More details in Appendix A, section 3.4.  
Pyrolysis oil  
The Pyrolysis oil is assumed to be produced from Swedish wood chips with 50% moisture (material from 
Ecoinvent 3.0 database).  Prior to pyrolysis the wood chips are pre-treated by drying them to around 10% 
moisture content and grind them into fine particles <6mm (Irribarren et al., 2012). After pre-treatment the wood 
particles are pyrolysed in a fluidized bed pyrolyser at 500 C by heating it with sand (Irribarren et al., 2012; 
Steele et al., 2012; Fan et al., 2011). Besides the pyrolysis oil the process also yields bio char and syngas, which 
are both burned to provide heat for the pyrolysis process (Steele et al., 2012; Fan et al., 2011). Hence there are 
no net outputs of these materials from the process. More details in Appendix A, section 3.5.  
Combustion & emission processes 
The inventories for the combustion processes for the various fuels at power plants are all based on modified 
fossil fuel combustion process from the Ecoinvent 3.0 database. The specific processes used were “Heat, district 
or industrial, natural gas {Europe without Switzerland}| heat production, natural gas, at industrial furnace low-
NOx >100kW | Conseq, U” for biogas, and “Heat, district or industrial, other than natural gas {Europe without 
Switzerland}| heat production, heavy fuel oil, at industrial furnace 1MW | Conseq, U” for the oil alternatives.  
The processes were changed to fit the bio-based SFs by scaling them to 1 MJ input energy of the fuels based on 
the calorific values (Appendix A, table A.1). Also the electricity production efficiencies were replaced with 
those for the specific power plants and set as a co-product, since the main function of the SFs is to start up the 
boilers. Lastly emissions were adjusted according to findings from literature as seen in table 2.2 under the 
assumption that the emissions were related to the amount of energy input, not amount of produced electricity. 
Also all emissions connected specifically to fossil fuels such as heavy metals were removed for both the gas and 
oil alternatives. Hence it was assumed that the combustion process was similar for all the crude vegetable oils 
including pyrolysis oil, and for all the biodiesels respectively, while the other fuel types had separate combustion 
processes.

2.3.4. Life cycle impact assessment  
The Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) translate the inventory data (i.e. exchanges of emissions and 
resources with the environment) into potential impact indicator scores at either midpoint or endpoint through a 
cause-effect chain and a set of characterisation factors (CF), determining how much impact a given emissions 
will potentially cause (SAIC, 2006). The characterisation factors are dependent on the chosen LCIA framework. 
In this study the ReCiPe framework was used, which has 18 midpoint categories as seen in table 2.3, and three 
endpoint impact categories also called the three areas of protection; human health measured in DALY (disability 
adjusted life years), ecosystems measured in species.yr (a measure of extinction rate), and resources measured in 
surplus cost (i.e. decrease in welfare), (also see Appendix A, figure A6).  
Table 2.3: Midpoint impact categories and their units of the ReCiPe framework

In the ReCiPe framework the midpoint categories regarding land occupation and land transformation do not have 
any characterisation factors, which is the same as using a CF of 1, and the method is therefore considered not to 
be solid for these impact categories in literature (Hauschild et al, 2013). Therefore these impact categories are in 
this study replaced with the impact category “Land use” from the ILCD (International Reference Life Cycle Data 
System) framework, which is measured in the unit “kg C deficit” and reflects the amount of carbon sequestration 
potential eliminated by using the land.   
==PAGE:17==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
11 
 
Normalisation 
The characterised impact scores at both midpoint and endpoint level all have different units (table 2.3) and are 
difficult to interpret in order to get an overview of a product’s overall environmental impact. The results can 
therefore be normalised by dividing them with an average person’s annual impact in each category, also called 
the normalisation reference. The normalisation reference builds on normalisation inventories, which are records 
of all emissions/extractions in a region per year (Ponsien, 2014). This study uses the default perspective 
(hierarchist) and the ReCiPe world normalisation reference, since global processes are used in the inventories.    
The normalisation step yields normalised impact scores in the unit person-equivalents (PE), which gives an idea 
of the magnitude of the impacts compared to that of an average person. The normalisation step also makes the 
impact categories comparable because they now have the same unit. However, the normalisation requires ample 
coverage of the normalisation inventories, i.e. all relevant impacts should be included in each category, 
otherwise the normalisation reference will be too low and as a consequence the product’s contribution will be 
overestimated (Ponsioen, 2014). In the ReCiPe framework such an incomplete normalisation reference is found 
for toxic impact categories, especially those of freshwater and marine ecotoxicity, because not all chemicals 
produced and released are recorded and because their faith in the environment is not comprehensively known. 
Hence for normalised impact scores the freshwater- and marine ecotoxicity impacts are overestimated, which 
should be taken into account when seeing results with large marine and freshwater ecotoxicity impacts (Laurent 
and Hauschild, 2015). This does not mean that the impacts should be disregarded, but rather that they should not 
overshadow other impacts despite large normalised scores. When normalising the impact scores a large 
normalisation reference gives a seemingly smaller impact from a product, but it does not mean an impact is not 
important just because the average person has a large resource consumption/emission and the impact therefore 
seems small. Hence the normalisation step does not define which impacts are important, but merely how large an 
impact is compared to the average person’s annual impact. 
Weighting 
To compare the various impacts directly, the normalised impact scores can be weighted by multiplying with a 
unit-less weighting factor (WF) that expresses the relative importance of the impacts (Brilhuis-Meijer, 2014).  
The weighting step is only applied at the endpoint level in the ReCiPe framework. The weighted scores are given 
the unit “points” or Pt, which similarly to the normalised share reflects the analysed product’s share of the 
annual average impact per person. Mega points (106) and milli points (10-3) are used for large and small scores 
respectively (Sustainable Minds, 2014). The weighting step has the advantage that the scores in different 
categories can be directly compared and added into one single environmental score, which makes interpretation 
and comparison easy between various products (Brilhuis-Meijer, 2014). Despite these advantages the endpoint 
scores carry larger uncertainties due to the extra interpretation step, and therefore the characterised scores at 
midpoint level is applied for all fuels in this study to understand where the impacts come from and avoid 
environmental trade-offs. The weighting step is very controversial since it entails a subjective valuation of the 
relative importance of the impacts, but by not using the weighting factors on the normalised scores, weighting 
factors of 1 are implicitly assumed, which also a subjective interpretation.  
2.3.5. Limitations 
The LCA results in this study build on consequential data, since they are intended to show the difference 
between using the various SFs. Therefore the results can be used for comparison only, and not to assess the 
absolute environmental impacts of the fuels. The main reason for this is that system expansion is used in case of 
==PAGE:18==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
12 
 
co-products and for most of the fuels this results in overall negative impact scores in some categories, which 
should not be interpreted as using the fuel gives environmental benefits per se, but that the specific impact is 
smaller compared to the impact of the credited co-product. I.e. the electricity produced when using SFs is 
modelled as a co-product using the Danish marginal data for electricity production, which is fossil based, and 
therefore gives rise to overall negative impact scores. Further the power plant data and the use of SFs in general 
is based on dust-fired boilers, but not fluidised bed boiler (FBB), where the dust id mixed with ash particles in an 
air flow. Hence the study applies only to boilers with the coal burners as described in Appendix B. The company 
level assessment is specific to the amount of SFs used in DONG Energy and therefore the results are limited to 
DONG Energy only, whereas conclusions at fuel level and plant level can be generalised to other power plants. 
2.3.6. Uncertainties 
The scoping of the study entails some uncertainty regarding the criteria and shortlisting of the alternative SFs. 
For one thing the criteria are uncertain themselves due to the lack of experimental data and knowledge of which 
specific combination properties is needed of SFs. Some alternative SFs assessed might therefore not be 
applicable as SFs at all for technical reasons. Also the market-related criteria are uncertain because the study is 
forward-looking and include fuels that are not marketed yet, but seems promising according to literature. 
However these fuels might not be produced at industrial level and needed amounts in the future. Regarding the 
scoping of the LCA itself, important processes in the production of fuels might be overlooked due to lacking 
information. If such a process was common to all the compared fuels it would most likely not affect the results, 
however if such a process was left out for one or few of the fuels it would change the results. To minimise this 
uncertainty as much as possible a literature review was made for the studied fuels to have data and process 
information from various sources and avoid overlooking any processes.  
The inventories are all based on literature and Ecoinvent processes, which all carry some uncertainty. The 
Ecoinvent processes and the literature on well tested technologies are all considered reliable and having low 
uncertainty. However for the less developed technologies, such as the algae cultivation, the data builds on many 
assumptions, and therefore sensitivity assessments are made by using data from a different source (Ajayebi et al., 
2013) than the one primarily used in the study (Stephenson et al., 2010). For the other fuels the assumptions with 
the largest uncertainty and influence on the results, are treated in sensitivity assessments as well. These include 
for the biogas: 1) the assumption of maize silage in the inputmix was assessed by modelling with grass silage 
and straw instead, 2) the assumption liquid CO2 production in biogas upgrading was tested by model the CO2 
release to the atmosphere instead, 3) the manure storage emissions were tested by changing them 10%.  For the 
oil fuels the following sensitivity assessments were made by testing the effect of the assumptions: 1) that long-
term impacts should be left out, by comparing results with and without long-term impacts 2) that the glycerine 
consequential process should be used, by using the allocation process 3) that Jatropha is grown in India by 
modelling an Indonesian production scenario, 4) that pyrolysis oil is made from wood by modelling straw as 
feedstock.  
For the uncertainties related to the LCIA framework the avoided glycerine production in the transesterification 
process was modelled with the allocation framework process, because the consequential system expansion was 
used where it made no sense, and resulted in positive impacts from the avoided glycerine production. The LCIA 
framework carries uncertainties in normalisation and weighting factors as well, however, since the same factors 
are used for all fuels and the study is for comparison purposes only, these uncertainties will be the same in all 
systems, and most are therefore not considered significant.  
==PAGE:19==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
13 
 
3. Results and discussion 
3.1. 
Gas system  
3.1.1. Fuel level 
To make a hotspot assessment and understand which processes are contributing to the impacts of each fuel, the 
midpoint characterized scores are shown for 1 m3 natural gas (NG) and 1 m3 biogas (BG) as percentage 
contribution 
from 
each 
production 
process 
in 
graph 
3.1 
and 
3.2, 
respectively.
 
Graph 0.1: Midpoint characterized scores for natural gas. Percentage contribution of processes 
The natural gas has a recurring pattern where the offshore extraction process in Norway is the main contributor 
to all the impact categories, followed by the long distance pipeline transportation. The first is caused by energy 
consumption and use of chemicals in the extraction process offshore, except for in the fossil depletion, where the 
main contribution is from using the natural gas itself, and only secondly from using energy and chemicals in the 
extraction process.  The transportation process consists mainly of energy for pressurising the natural gas grid.  
For biogas the anaerobic digestion causes the largest impact in most categories due to emissions to the air. The 
inputmix also plays a significant role, which is primarily caused by the farming of maize silage. The upgrading 
process on the other hand contributes negatively to most of the impacts because of the consequential modelling 
framework, which entails that the liquid CO2 production is modelled as an avoided product, which would 
otherwise be produced form fossil resources. This crediting is enough to offset the positive impacts of the 
electricity used in the upgrading process, making the overall contribution negative. The avoided manure storage 
also contributes to the negative side of the impacts in five of the categories due to the emissions that are avoided 
by not having to store the manure. The grid transportation is less significant for the biogas than the natural gas, 
because it includes only the short distance grid transportation in Denmark as opposed to the natural gas.

Master thesis at DTU

Graph 0.2: Midpoint characterized scores for biogas. Percentage contribution of processes 
Both the maize silage used in the inputmix and the crediting for liquid CO2-production in the upgrading process 
has large influence on the results. Both assumptions are based on literature (Jacobsen et al., 2013; Schmidt et al., 
2013) and best practices, but in Jacobsen et al. (2013) grass and straw are mentioned as alternatives to the maize 
silage, and in none of the studies is liquid CO2-production considered. Due to the large influence and uncertainty 
of the assumptions, sensitivity assessments are made for both.  
For the inputmix two alternative scenarios are investigated; one where grass silage replaces the 10% maize 
silage, and one where straw does. For both scenarios it is assumed that the dry matter content is the same, and 
that the anaerobic digestion efficiency is maintained. The Ecoinvent 3.0 database is used for the inventories of 
both the grass and the straw. The grass silage scenario generally results in larger impacts, whereas the straw 
scenario results in lower impacts for biogas production at both midpoint and endpoint levels (Appendix A, graph 
A31-A33). Especially the toxicity and eutrophication categories are affected. The effect of using another 
feedstock for these 10% of the inputmix proves to be important for the environmental performance of the biogas. 
Hence from an environmental point of view biogas produced from a manure-straw mix should be chosen over 
biogas from a manure-maize mix, which is again preferable compared to biogas from a manure-grass mix.   
For the upgrading process the liquid CO2 production is considered best practice, but no evidence has been found 
in literature that it is also the most widespread practice, which makes the assumption uncertain. The alternative 
scenario is that the CO2 removed from the biogas is released directly to the atmosphere. The result of this 
scenario is positive impacts from the purification process (Appendix A, graph A34), which entirely eliminates 
the negative impacts in some categories. The difference is especially seen in the climate change, eutrophication, 
and depletion categories, due to the fossil energy for liquid CO2 production that is no longer avoided. Since the 
change in impact scores are quite significant, it should always be preferred to use biogas from a plant that uses 
the CO2 removed from the biogas to produce liquid CO2 whenever possible.

A final sensitivity assessment was made of the manure storage emissions because it causes significant impacts in 
a few of the categories, and the data might hold some uncertainty. Since the data comes from primary sources of 
manure composition the uncertainty is not expected to be large, and the alternative scenario considered is 
therefore a 10% decrease in N-content and thus emissions per kg manure input. This resulted in only very small 
changes (appendix A section 5.1.3.), which are not considered significant.   
3.1.2. Plant level 
Two power plants in DONG Energy use natural gas as SF; HEV and AVV2. The characterised midpoint impacts 
are negative in most categories due to the crediting of useful energy produced from the SFs; 0.038 kWh 
electricity at AVV2, 0.019 kWh electricity and 0.384 MJ heat at HEV (Appendix G). A comparison of the two 
plants based on burning 1 MJ natural gas is seen in Appendix A, table A13. In this comparison HEV has lower 
impacts in all but four midpoint categories. This difference is caused by the greater useful energy production at 
HEV, caused by the possibility of heat production even when there is no electricity production. This is possible 
because the high pressure turbine can be by-passed, and hence heat can be produced before the steam pressure is 
high enough to produce electricity. At AVV2 only electricity is produced during SF use, and hence only 
electricity is credited. It is obvious that the more useful energy that is produced from the SFs, the lower the 
environmental impacts in general. The by-passing of the high pressure turbine optimises the useful energy 
production during SF use, making it a preferred technology in terms of environmental optimisation of SFs.  
At plant level NG and BG can also be compared directly. The midpoint comparison (Appendix A, table A14) 
shows that the biogas has lower impacts in most categories; however the eutrophication and water toxicity 
impacts are higher for the biogas as well as water depletion and land use, which are all categories generally 
associated with farming. The impacts from natural gas are larger in especially climate change, human and 
terrestrial toxicity and depletion categories. Since this comparison is not conclusive, the weighted endpoint 
scores are applied, as seen in graph 3.3. The negative results in the graph are caused by the consequential 
modelling of co-products as avoided products, such as the electricity in the combustion. The graph shows that 
the resource depletion caused by the natural gas is important for this single-score evaluation. All in all the biogas 
single-score is negative and thereby it performs better than the natural gas for which the single score is positive. 
In both cases HEV has a lower impact than AVV2 of approximately 5% for natural gas and 10% for biogas.

3.1.3. Company level 
At company level the FU is assessed, i.e. burning 1519 TJ gaseous SF at HEV and AVV2 according to their 
average gas consumption in 2013 and 2014 (Appendix F). At Company level the biogas still performs in most 
midpoint categories (appendix A, table A and graph A7). For the endpoint characterised scores biogas has the 
lowest impact in all three endpoint categories; resources, human health, and ecosystems (appendix A, table 
A16), and the endpoint single score calculated from the weighted endpoint impacts is overall negative with a 
value of          -0.7 MPt, whereas it is positive for natural gas with a value of 7.6 MPt (Appendix A, graph A8). 
At this aggregated level the conclusion thus is that biogas is environmentally preferable compared to natural gas. 
The overall negative impacts arise because the impact of electricity and co-products credited exceeds the impacts 
of the production processes, which is a characteristic of the consequential framework modelling approach.  
In order to assess how the uncertainties that were found for biogas affects this conclusion, the endpoint single 
score for each alternative production pathway is investigated as shown in graph 3.4. The natural gas has the 
largest score, which is primarily made up of the resources impact and secondly of the human health impact, 
whereas the ecosystems impact has very little influence. The original case of biogas (i.e. including liquid CO2 
crediting and based on maize silage) has a negative score for the resources impact, but it is still dominating the 
single endpoint score, which is overall negative despite the positive impacts in the other two endpoint categories. 
 
Graph 0.4: Endpoint single scores form weighted scores of natural gas vs. the five biogas scenarios 
When looking at the endpoint scores for the sensitivity assessments, it is very much in line with the results at 
fuel level. The decreased manure emissions scenario is only 8% larger than the original biogas scenario (-0.5 
MPt), and hene the final result is not sensitive to the manure emission.  In the two scenarios of biogas where 
liquid CO2 is not produced, and where grass is used instead of maize, the impact becomes overall positive with 
values of 2.3 MPt and 1.7 MPt, respectively. Hence grass as feedstock and upgrading without liquid CO2 
production, should not be preferred if any of the other biogas options are available. However, both of these 
scenarios still have lower single endpoint scores than the natural gas. The most preferable biogas scenario is that 
where straw is used as feedstock instead of maize silage together with the manure. This result in a single score of 
-1.9 MPt, which is three times lower than the original biogas scenario because the straw is a waste product and 
the avoided waste treatment of straw thus contributes to the negative impact.

It should be noted though, that these endpoint scores gives a very high level assessment, where trade-offs 
between different impacts are not taken into account. For instance even though the straw-based inputmix might 
yield the lowest scores in human health and resources, the impact in ecosystems is actually larger than for the 
original biogas scenario, and only the grass-based biogas as larger ecosystems impact. Hence, the weighting 
might put emphasis to one impact category more than another, which should be taken into account when looking 
at the highly aggregated results in graph 3.4.  
3.2. 
Oil system 
3.2.1. Fuel level 
In order to determine the hotspots in the production of the SF oil alternatives, a percentage distribution of the 
contribution from each production process was calculated for each fuel, as seen for Jatropha biodiesel in graph 
3.5.  Graphs of both midpoint and endpoint scores for all SFs can be found in Appendix A, section 4.2.1.  
 
Graph 0.5: Percentage distribution of production processes’ contribution to the characterised impact scores for Jatropha 
biodiesel. Similar graphs for the other fuels in Appendix A, section 4.2.1.  
In the case of Jatropha oil and –biodiesel production the cultivation step is dominating most of the impact 
categories, some of them with a negative score due to the assumption of electricity production from seed husk. 
The negative oil extraction impacts in especially the water-related categories stem from the crediting of fertiliser 
potential in the seed press cake. For the Jatropha biodiesel the transesterification contributes to especially the 
toxicity impacts due to the energy and chemicals used. The assessment is based on data from India, and therefore 
a sensitivity assessment was made using Indonesian data instead, which gave almost no difference in results (2% 
in endpoint score), and was considered insignificant from which of these two countries the Jatropha oil 
originated (Appendix A, section 5.2.3).  
For the UCO the contribution graph looks different (Appendix A, section 4.2.1) with the purification process 
dominating the UCO and the transesterification process dominating the UCO biodiesel in most categories. There 
are no impacts from the oil production itself, since UCO is considered a waste product, and therefore assumed to 
have no upstream impacts. This assumption can actually be said to be an allocation, where 100% of the upstream 
-100%
-80%
-60%
-40%
-20%
0%
20%
40%
60%
80%
100%
Cultivation
Oil extraction
Transesterification
==PAGE:24==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
18 
 
impacts are allocated to the primary function of the oil, which is the deep frying. This approach is chosen 
because it is assumed that it is the demand for the primary function (i.e. oil for cooking) that is controlling the 
market 100%, and hence that the oil production does not increase due to demand for UCO as fuel. Another 
reason for not associating any impacts with the oil itself is that the alternative pathways for the UCO, if it was 
not used as fuel, are not known. One scenario could be waste treatment to dispose of it another could be the 
production of some useful product, e.g. soap. In either case this alternative pathway should be modelled as an 
avoided process. In the waste treatment scenario that would mean net avoidance of impacts. In the product 
scenario it would mean that this product (e.g. the soap) should then be produced from something else, which 
could cause either net positive or negative impacts. Due to the uncertainty in alternative pathways the zero 
impact approach is thus taken.  
The generally low impacts from the UCO itself lead to the transesterification dominating most impact categories 
for the UCO biodiesel. The impacts of transesterification can for the most part be traced back to the crediting for 
glycerine production, Which would otherwise be produced form allyl-chloride and yield HCL as co-product, and 
it is the crediting of HCL that gives rise to positive impact scores from the glycerine credited, rather than 
negative as expected (appendix A, graph A42). This contradiction arises because of the systematic use of system 
expansion in the consequential database, also in cases like this where it gives rise to doubtful results. Therefore, 
as sensitivity scenario, allocation is used according to the ISO hierarchy of multi-functional processes, where 
system expansion is not possible. The allocation is used only for the allyl-chloride process to avoid the HCL 
crediting. This changes the results, since glycerine crediting in the transesterification process then contributes 
negatively to all impact categories but water depletion, and hence the entire impact profile for the 
transesterification process changes. This will be the case for all biodiesel types, but the change is more 
significant for UCO, since the transesterification dominates its impacts so largely.  
For the algal oil and algal biodiesel the cultivation and dewatering contribute significantly in all categories with 
positive scores due to the energy, fertiliser, and chemicals used in these processes (Appendix A, graph A19). The 
oil extraction contributes negatively due to the crediting of energy production from algal residues. For the algal 
biodiesel the transesterification process has very little influence as opposed to the Jatropha and UCO biodiesel 
due to the larger impacts from the other algae production processes.  
Since algae as fuel is a relatively new technology, there are still large uncertainties regarding the data for 
production processes and therefore large variation in reported inventories in literature. To illustrate this variation 
the impact scores for an alternative inventory from Ajayebi et al. (2013) is held constant at 100% in all impact 
categories and shown in graph 3.6 relative to the original results from Stephenson et al. (2010). 
==PAGE:25==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
19 
 
 
Graph 0.6: comparison of characterised impact scores of 1kg algal biodiesel with Ajayebi as 100% for all categories 
The difference between the two datasets for algae biodiesel (Ajayebi et al., 2013 and Stephenson et al., 2010) 
arises mainly from the cultivation process and the assumptions about algae concentration in the water. This scale 
of differences is not uncommon in literature (Bahardar and Khan, 2013) and the reason is that only few large 
scale algae production plants exists to generate reliable data, and many different algae species and growth 
conditions are currently investigated because it is a very new technology. Most results are therefore from lab-
scale experiments and inventories are therefore likely to change significantly when upscaling to commercial 
production (Bahadar and Khan, 2013). The algal oil as biofuel thus faces both economic and technical barriers to 
become a viable alternative to fossil fuels, and the changes will most likely also affect the environmental impact.  
It is therefore important to take into consideration the specific production conditions when choosing algal-based 
fuels to be certain of the environmental benefit.    
For the pyrolysis oil the fuel level assessment reveals that the production of the wood used as feedstock is the 
most important process for the overall impact due to the fossil fuels used in forestry processes. This is apparent 
for the midpoint level assessment and even more so at endpoint level, where the wood chip production causes 
especially large impact in the ecosystems category, which is the only fuel for which this category has been the 
largest. In order to test the assumption of wood as feedstock, a sensitivity assessment was made using straw as 
feedstock for the pyrolysis oil. This resulted in drastically changed impact scores, illustrated in graph 3.7 with 
the weighted impact scores. Here it is seen that the impacts are smaller for straw production, and even negative 
for the human health and resources categories. This is because straw is seen as a waste product, and therefore 
impacts are avoided by using it as fuel feedstock compared to burning it on the field or otherwise dispose of it. 
However, these impact scores are dependent on the alternate fate of the straw, e.g. bedding in stables or primary 
fuel at power plants, which is linked to the market of the straw, which is taken into consideration in the 
Ecoinvent process and assumed to be energy production.

Master thesis at DTU

Graph 0.7: weighted endpoint scores at fuel level of 1 kg pyrolysis oil from wood chips and straw 
For the fossil alternative, the heavy fuel oil, the oil extraction process and refinery are the most contributing 
processes by far in all categories. This is caused by the fossil energy and to some extent the chemicals used in 
the onshore petroleum production from Russia and other countries. 
3.2.2. Plant level 
At plant level the combustion process is included in the assessment as 1 MJ input energy of each type of fuel 
burned at each power plant. This allows for comparison of the plants as well as the fuels. For all of the oil SFs 
the plant performance when burning 1 MJ follows the same pattern: the higher electricity production the lower 
environmental impacts (Appendix A, section 4.2.2). Therefore the lowest impacts are seen for ASV2 followed 
by ASV5, SSV, ESV, and lastly AVV1 with the highest impacts and zero electricity production. This tendency is 
seen because all the emissions from the combustion are assumed to be dependent on the fuel input energy and an 
Ecoinvent process is used as basis, making the emissions similar between the plants. Hence the largest difference 
lies in how much electricity can be credited. Based on these assumptions the electricity production (and heat 
production) should always be sought increased in the load zones where SFs are used.  
However, these assumptions do not take into account the flexibility of the plants, which is defined by the 
percentage of full load where the boiler is able to run on the primary fuel alone. The flexibility can vary between 
at least 15-30% (i.e. at 15-30% of the full load, the primary fuel can be used alone) and hence determines how 
long time the SF is needed during a start-up. In the case of high boiler flexibility (i.e. the SF can be taken out at 
around 15%), the electricity credited per MJ SF might be low, because the SF is only used (always together with 
primary fuel) at the lowest temperatures, where no electricity is produced. On the other hand, if the flexibility is 
high (e.g. around 30%), the SF is used up to 30% of the full load, where electricity production might primarily 
take place at loads above 20%, hence making the average credit per MJ SF higher. When assessing the SF use 
only as in this study, this would make the low-flexibility plant seem to have a better environmental performance. 
However, if the primary fuel has lower environmental impacts than the SF, the high-flexibility plant would 
actually be preferable, since the primary fuel is used early, limiting the use of SF. If the primary fuel on the other 
hand has high environmental impacts compared to the SF, it would be preferable from an environmental point of

From table 3.1 a general pattern can be seen where HFO have the highest impacts in most categories, followed 
closely by the algal biodiesel, whereas Jatropha oil and UCO seem to have the lowest impacts in most categories. 
In the freshwater eutrophication, however, HFO has the lowest impact score of all the fuels, because no 
phosphate (P) is used in the production as opposed to almost all plant-related feedstock.  In the water depletion 
HFO also performs well, since only little water is needed for fossil oil production compared to plant material, 
which needs watering. The only feedstock performing better is UCO, which is a waste product and hence has no 
plant production included in the life cycle. The water depletion and land use, which are needed for plant 
cropping, are therefore also the impacts where Jatropha oil and biodiesel has highest impacts. 
==PAGE:28==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU 
 
22 
 
Also it is observed that the biodiesel in all three cases seem to have higher impacts than the crude oil equivalent, 
because it requires an extra processing step to refine the oils to biodiesel and achieve fuel properties closer to 
those of fossil diesel. However, biodiesel was developed to fit diesel engine cars, whereas the SF used currently 
at most power plants is heavy fuel oil, which is not a diesel-type fuel, and therefore the characteristics of 
biodiesel might not even be necessary at the power plants. This would mean that the transesterification process 
could be avoided entirely in the SF production, thereby reducing the environmental impacts. Hence only if the 
biodiesel characteristics are needed for the SF, should biodiesel be used. Otherwise crude oils should be 
preferred, which is most likely to also be cheaper and thus make a better business case.  
3.2.3. Company level  
At company level the fuels are compared based on the FU, which equals 423 TJ input energy from SFs in total at 
all power plants. The characterised midpoint scores for the fuels resembles those form the plant level but scaled 
to 423 TJ rather than 1 MJ input. When aggregating the impacts at endpoint level and including normalisation 
and weighting, it results in the single endpoint scores seen in graph 3.8.  The weighted endpoint scores support 
the conclusion that HFO has the by far largest impact of all the investigated alternatives, and that Jatropha oil has 
the lowest impact. However, the ranking of fuels in between is not the same as on plant level. Also, the general 
pattern that biodiesel had higher impact than the crude oil alternative is no longer valid at this aggregated level. 
These differences are caused by two factors; firstly the impact categories assessed are different at endpoint that 
at midpoint; secondly the results are weighted, which change the relative importance of the impact categories.   
 
Graph 0.8: Single weighted endpoint scores for the HFO and seven alternative SFs 
The ranking of the fuels for the endpoint scores is that HFO has largest impact followed by pyrolysis oil, algal 
oil, algal biodiesel, UCO, UCO biodiesel, Jatropha biodiesel, and lastly Jatropha oil with the lowest impact. The 
impact from the HFO is mostly from resource depletion and human health impact due to climate change 
(Appendix A, graph A29). For the pyrolysis oil the impact comes more or less equally from all three endpoint 
categories (Human health, ecosystems, and resources).  
For the algal oil and -biodiesel the endpoint scores are almost equal because the transesterification process does 
not contribute significantly compared to the oil production processes. For both algae-fuels the impact comes 
from human health and resources impact with only very little from ecosystems. The only fuels with lower

impacts in ecosystems are the UCO-fuels. Here the difference between the UCO and the UCO biodiesel is larger 
than for the algae-fuels both in relative and absolute terms. For both alge- and UCO-fuels the biodiesel performs 
better. Most of this difference comes from the resources impact, and in the midpoint assessment the fossil 
depletion is actually one of the only categories where UCO biodiesel performs better than UCO. This is also the 
case for the Algae and algae biodiesel, however here the difference is much smaller as is also the case for the 
endpoint score. This shows that the resource impact, especially the fossil depletion, is very determining for the 
single endpoint score.  
The fact that the biodiesel has lower impact despite the extra production process is caused by the crediting of co-
products. Hence the assumptions of glycerine and fertiliser production from transesterification process are 
crucial for this result. For Jatropha, as opposed to the other two biodiesel types, only glycerine and not fertiliser 
is produced form the transesterification process, and therefore the crude oil performs better than the biodiesel 
also at endpoint level. Since the fossil depletion has such a large influence, and it depends on the assumption that 
seed husk is burned for energy recovery, this assumption is crucial to the final conclusion of Jatropha’s 
environmental impact.  
Even though the single endpoint scores are easier to comprehend and evaluate, they also hold higher uncertainty 
due to the normalisation and weighting steps. Another way to evaluate the fuel is to look at the climate change 
impact (kg CO2 eq) and the fossil depletion impact (kg oil eq), which are relevant due to the large focus in 
politics worldwide and the large impact on the single score form these two categories. The midpoint 
characterised scores for these two categories are shown in graph 3.9 a+b.  
 
Graph 0.9 a+b: Characterised impact score for the chosen categories Fossil depletion and Climate change. 
The pattern in these two midpoint categories in terms of relative impact sizes is the same as the pattern of the 
single weighted endpoint scores in graph 3.8, with the algal oil and –biodiesel as only exception. This 
resemblance shows the great influence from these two categories on the final results, where climate change 
contribute to the human health impact and fossil depletion to the resources impact in the endpoint scores.

4. Conclusions and recommendations 
To provide an overview of bio-based fuels suited as SFs a list of criteria was developed based on information 
from dust-fired power plants provided by DONG Energy. This resulted in one the following alternatives: 
Alternative for natural gas:  
Alternatives for heavy fuel oil HFO:  
-Upgraded biogas delivered via the natural gas grid 
-Pyrolysis oil from Swedish wood chips 
 
-UCO collected in Denmark 
 
-UCO biodiesel, collected and produced in Denmark 
 
-Jatropha oil from India 
 
-Jatropha biodiesel from India 
 
-Algal oil produced in Denmark 
 
-Algal biodiesel produced in Denmark 
The criteria carried uncertainties for especially the technical parameters, so to test whether the shortlisted fuels 
are actually suitable as SFs and how well they perform, they should be tested under real conditions at power 
plants. The comparative LCA made for the shortlisted fuels showed that all of the bio-based SFs had an overall 
lower environmental impact than the fossils. The results proved to be very dependent on the feedstock used, both 
for the biogas where straw in the inputmix had lower impact than maize, and for the oil alternatives where straw 
instead of wood for pyrolysis oil had lower impacts and for the crude oils and biodiesel, where the waste 
feedstock UCO had lower impact than energy crop feedstock algae and Jatropha. For all assessed cases, 
however, the bio-based SFs performed better than the fossils, the only exception being the alternative algae 
cultivation assessed. From an environmental point of view the possibilities for changing fossil SFs to bio-based 
should therefore be investigated with the imperative influence of feedstock choice I mind. Especially the 
emerging algae technologies entail large uncertainties, and therefore more research is needed to map the actual 
cultivation in- and outputs and the viability of large scale algae cultivation.  
The LCA at plant level showed that plant performance is highly dependent on useful energy production. In 
general it is therefore recommended to optimise energy production at during SF use, for instance by bypassing of 
the high pressure steam turbine in CHPs to produce heat when demand is present. Such optimisation might 
already be the practice at most CHPs of economic reasons. At DONG Energy specifically it was found that of 
the plants using natural gas HEV perform better environmentally than AVV2 due to such a bypass mechanism. 
For the plants using HFO as SF the environmental performance dependent solely on electricity generation from 
SFs, and from lowest to highest impacts the ranking of the plants is: ASV2, ASV5, SSV, ESV, AVV1. Hence to 
optimise the company impact from SFs the cause of the difference could be identified and solutions from the 
best performing plants could be implemented on the others to increase sustainability.  
At company level in DONG energy the ranking of oil SFs from low to high impacts is: Jatropha oil, jatropha 
biodiesel, UCO biodiesel, UCO, algal biodiesel, algal oil, and HFO with the by far largest impact. The end 
results depended highly on specifically the climate change impact and fossil depletion impact, which are easily 
affected by crediting of co-products. To be certain of the environmental benefit the specific feedstock and 
production methods therefore needs to be known. For all investigated cases the bio-based SFs were preferable.

only in the main report. The appendix contains a separate list of references to give a better overview of 
references used specifically for the LCA.

Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants
