1. 
Goal 
1.1. 
Intended applications of the deliverables / results  
This study aims to:  
- 
Provide an overview of alternative bio-based support fuels (SFs) for dust fired power plants 
- 
Assess environmental hotspots of SF production to support decisions of specific production pathways 
- 
Compare SFs at plant level to determine the influence of power plant specifications on environmental 
performance regarding SFs, while comparing the SFs and ranking them according to their performance 
- 
Assess the overall environmental impacts of changing from fossil to bio-based SFs at aggregated 
company level, to specifically support decisions in DONG Energy (DE) 
1.2. 
Limitations due to the method, assumptions, and impact coverage 
The study compares the environmental impacts of different SFs at midpoint and endpoint level, based on the 
consequential LCA modelling framework. The consequential modelling entails that the results can be used for 
comparison only, and not to assess the absolute environmental impacts of the fuels. The main reason for this is 
that system expansion is used in case of co-products and for most of the fuels this results in overall negative 
impact scores in some categories, which should not be interpreted as using the fuel gives environmental benefits 
per se, but that the specific impact is smaller compared to the impact of the credited co-product. Further the 
power plant data and the use of SFs in general is based on dust-fired boilers, but not fluidised bed boiler (FBB), 
where the dust id mixed with ash particles in an air flow. Hence the study applies only to boilers with the coal 
burners as described in Appendix B. The company level assessment is specific to the amount of SFs used in 
DONG Energy and therefore the results are limited to DONG Energy only, whereas conclusions from fuel level 
and plant level can be generalised to other power plants. 
The scoping of the study entails some uncertainty regarding the criteria and shortlisting of the alternative SFs. 
For one thing the criteria are uncertain themselves due to the lack of experimental data and knowledge of which 
specific combination properties is needed of SFs. Some alternative SFs assessed might therefore not be 
applicable as SFs at all for technical reasons. Also the market-related criteria are uncertain because the study is 
forward-looking and include fuels that are not marketed yet, but seems promising according to literature. 
However these fuels might not be produced at industrial level and needed amounts in the future. Regarding the 
scoping of the LCA itself, important processes in the production of fuels might be overlooked due to lacking 
information. If such a process was common to all the compared fuels it would most likely not affect the results, 
however if such a process was left out for one or few of the fuels it would change the results. To minimise this 
uncertainty as much as possible a literature review was made for the studied fuels to have data and process 
information from various sources and avoid overlooking any processes.  
The inventories are all based on literature and Ecoinvent processes, which all carry some uncertainty. The 
Ecoinvent processes and the literature on well tested technologies are all considered reliable and having low 
uncertainty. However for the less developed technologies, such as the algae cultivation, the data builds on many 
assumptions, and therefore sensitivity assessments are made by using data from a different source (Ajayebi et al., 
2013) than the one primarily used in the study (Stephenson et al., 2010). For the other fuels the assumptions with 
==PAGE:38==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
4 
 
the largest uncertainty and influence on the results, are treated in sensitivity assessments as well. These include 
for the biogas: 1) the assumption of maize silage in the inputmix was assessed by modelling with grass silage 
and straw instead, 2) the assumption liquid CO2 production in biogas upgrading was tested by model the CO2 
release to the atmosphere instead, 3) the manure storage emissions were tested by changing them 10%.  For the 
oil fuels the following sensitivity assessments were made by testing the effect of the assumptions: 1) that long-
term impacts should be left out, by comparing results with and without long-term impacts 2) that the glycerine 
consequential process should be used, by using the allocation process 3) that Jatropha is grown in India by 
modelling an Indonesian production scenario, 4) that pyrolysis oil is made from wood by modelling straw as 
feedstock.  
For the uncertainties related to the LCIA framework the avoided glycerine production in the transesterification 
process was modelled with the allocation framework process, because the consequential system expansion was 
used where it made no sense, and resulted in positive impacts from the avoided glycerine production. The LCIA 
framework carries uncertainties in normalisation and weighting factors as well, however, since the same factors 
are used for all fuels and the study is for comparison purposes only, these uncertainties will be the same in all 
systems, and most are therefore not considered significant.  
1.3. 
Reasons for carrying out the study and decision-context   
The study is carried out to support decisions regarding the raw material strategy for SFs at central DONG Energy 
power plants to aid in reaching the goal of 50% biomass based fuels at DONG Energy’s power plants in 2020 
(DONG Energy, 2014 a). The decision-context of the study is thus a situation B ‘meso/macro level decision 
support’, since the decision to use on alternative support fuels could change the demand site of the Danish 
market of that fuel to an extent that additional production capacity is needed. Also the decision could change the 
Danish background system of heat and electricity production, since DONG Energy contributes around 30% of 
the annual Danish electricity production from their thermal power plants, and 30% of the district heating (Dong 
Energy, 2014 b; Energistyrelsen, 2014).  Further the results of the study support the same type of decisions in 
other energy companies, which might lead to changes at similar scale in other countries.  
1.4. 
Target audience  
The target audience is decision makers in DONG Energy, and other energy companies managing dust-fired 
power plants, responsible for defining the fuel strategy. Since the study is part of a thesis, the target audience 
also encompass the supervisor, censor and other external stakeholders technically interested in the study.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
5 
 
2. 
Scope Definition 
2.1. 
Primary function, functional unit, and reference flows  
In order to be able to compare the current and alternative options for SFs at DONG Energy power plants, it is 
important that the fuels assessed provide the same service to the system. This service is defined both 
quantitatively and qualitatively by the Functional Unit (FU): 
The necessary amount of support fuel that provides an all year-round ability to 1) ignite the primary dust fuel in 
the current DONG Energy CHP-plant set-up, and 2) be used as an emergency and peak load fuel on all relevant 
DONG Energy CHP-plants in an average year. 
- 
Necessary amount: 1519 TJ for natural gas (NG) substitutes / 423 TJ for heavy fuel oil (HFO) 
substitutes 
- 
Current set-up: shall have the ability to be handled by the current feeding mechanism 
- 
Relevant plants: plants using the same type of SF (i.e. either all HFO or all NG using plants) 
- 
In an average year: Based on an average of 2013-2014 data 
The amount of input energy needed from support fuels each year is based on average data from all DONG 
Energy power plants for 2013 and 2014, which can be seen in Appendix F. Each of the fuels can replace either 
the natural gas or he HFO, because the current installations on each power plant can only handle fuel in the same 
physical form as the current SF as described in Appendix D. Since it is not physically possible to use oily SFs on 
current natural gas systems and vice versa, it is not relevant to compare gaseous and liquid SFs to each other. 
The reference flow of each alternative SF is based on the necessary amount of SF and the lower heating value 
(LHV) of the fuels assessed. The total use of support fuel on all nine of DONG Energy’s power plants is 1942 
TJ, of which 1519 TJ is natural gas and 423 TJ is HFO. The reference flows of each SF are shown in table A1.  
Table A 1: Annual SF demand in DONG Energy based on average 2013-2014 data for NG and HFO substitutes, respectively.

By defining the functional unit as the provision of a specific amount of energy, as opposed to a specific amount 
of fuel in ton or m3, it is ensured that the same service is provided by each of the assessed fuels. In this way the 
same energy is available regardless of the different energy content and properties of each fuel, and therefore the 
reference flows in table A1 are different. The functional unit is aggregated to company level however 
==PAGE:40==

6 
 
assessments are made at plant level (for burning 1 MJ SF) and fuel level (Producing 1 kg/ 1m3 SF) as well to 
generalise the results.  
2.2. 
System boundaries 
The specific processes involved in production differ for each type of fuel and feedstock assessed. Therefore the 
specific system boundaries vary as well. However all system boundaries are cradle-to-grave perspective, 
including the extraction/cultivation, processing, and use phases with transport in between. To make as 
comprehensive an assessment as possible all infrastructure processes are included in the system boundaries, i.e. 
all machinery and facilities used for processing the fuels. Since system expansion is used to deal with 
multifunctional processes the co-products are included in the system boundaries as well.  
2.2.1. Biogas 
The system boundary shown in figure A1, fits Danish produced biogas produced from an average Danish mix of 
biogas feedstock (Jacobsen et al., 2013), which is optimised to be delivered via the Danish natural gas network.

2.3. 
LCI framework 
Since the decision context of the study is a situation B with decision support at meso/macro level, the 
consequential LCI framework is used as prescribed by the European Commission (European Commission, 
2010). This implies using marginal data, and for this purpose the Ecoinvent 3.0 consequential database is used. 
The SimaPro software is used in the technical modelling of the inventories to calculate the impacts scores. In the 
case of waste derived feedstock (i.e. UCO or manure for biogas) allocation is used as an exception with 100% of 
the upstream impacts allocated to the primary function of the system from which the waste is derived. Thus zero 
upstream impacts are associated with using the waste product as fuel feedstock.   
2.4. 
Multifunctional processes 
Multifunctional processes are treated by system expansion according to the consequential modelling framework 
(European Commission, 2010). Multifunctional processes occur in most of the production pathways for the 
various SFs, where useful co-products are produced that can replace conventionally produced products.  
Since the primary function of the SFs is to heat the boiler and ignite the main fuel in dust-fired boilers, the 
electricity and heat produced from the SFs are considered co-products as well and therefore treated by system 
expansion.  
 
 
==PAGE:45==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
11 
 
2.5. 
LCIA framework 
The LCIA framework used in this study is the ReCiPe version 1.11 of august 2014. The results are evaluated on 
both midpoint and endpoint level using the hierarchist cultural view, which is considered the default in the 
ReCiPe framework (ReCiPe, 2014). Further the results are normalized and weighted to add them into a single 
score, which is also performed with the ReCiPe methodology.  
The ReCiPe framework uses characterisation factors to convert the LCI results (inputs and outputs from 
processes) to the midpoint characterisation scores as seen in figure A6. The midpoint scores are then further 
extrapolated to the three endpoint scores, called the three areas of protection; “Human health” measured in 
DALY (disability-adjusted life year), “Ecosystems” measured in species year (a measure of extinction rate), and 
“Resources” measured in surplus cost (i.e. decrease in welfare) (ReCiPe, 2014).  
 
Figure A 6: ReCiPe framework illustration of midpoint and endpoint correlation with LCI results. 
At both midpoint and endpoint level the impact scores can be normalised, which is achieved by dividing with the 
amount of that impact per person per year (the normalisation reference). For this study the world reference is 
used for normalisation. The midpoint normalised scores however, does not have a robust calculation of land use/ 
occupation (Hauschild et al., 2013) and therefore the ILCD (International Reference Life Cycle Data System) 
LCIA framework is used for this impact category throughout the study.  
==PAGE:46==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
12 
 
It should be noted that even though the endpoint scores, especially the weighted impacts scores aggregated into 
one single value, might be easier to interpret  they also carry a larger uncertainty than the midpoint score due to 
the extra interpretation step in the cause-effect chain. Hence the endpoint single-value scores might be used to 
compare the alternative fuels, but the midpoint assessment is performed as well to understand where the impacts 
come from and how they arise in the life cycle.  
2.6. 
Representativeness of data 
The representativeness of the data describes the extent to which the inventory data is representative of what 
actually happens in the life cycle of the system. The representativeness is divided into three aspects; temporal, 
technological, and geographical representativeness. In the below sections the three aspects are described in terms 
of which degree of representativeness is needed to fulfil the goal of the study.  
2.6.1. Temporal representativeness 
The temporal scope of this study relates to the future and includes processes which are not yet operated on 
commercial scale. This apply to the production of 2nd, and especially 3rd, generation biofuels which are not 
commercially produced yet, but expected to be so in the near future, and thereby become viable alternative SFs. 
According to the ILCD handbook “the most recent data that is still valid for that to-be-represented time” should 
be used in the LCI to ensure temporal representativeness of such future processes (European Commission, 
2010). The data required is thus from large commercial-scale production of the various fuels.  
Another aspect of the temporal representativeness of the study is the inter-annual variations in the LCI data. This 
is especially important for power plants with their production being controlled by the temperature and hence 
varies with the hot and cold season. However, the variation over the year in use of SFs does not follow any 
specific pattern and vary for each power plant. To avoid any uncertainty based on seasonal variations the 
inventory data should include annual data or an average over more years, to make the annual production data 
representative for the study. Inventory data related directly to the production in DONG Energy should have high 
temporal representativeness to not be biased by any changes in SF use over the past years, since the amounts of 
SF directly determine the quantitative dimension of the functional unit.   
2.6.2. Technological representativeness 
The technological representativeness is closely related to the temporal representativeness because technologies 
can develop significantly over time. This is also the case in this study, since the future perspective means that the 
system that is described is not yet in place commercially. Hence the most recent data is not available from 
commercially scaled production, but only from lab-scale tests of producing different biofuels from different 
feedstock. Upscaling to commercial production might induce changes to the technology, and therefore the most 
recent data available should be used, preferably from pilot projects of large scale biofuel/feedstock production if 
it is possible.  
Another important aspect of the technological representativeness is that the study is a consequential LCA, and 
therefore inventories of the marginal data should be used in the foreground system. Inventory data form DONG 
Energy should also have a high technological representativeness to correctly describe the technologies used now 
or within the temporal scope of the study.  
==PAGE:47==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
13 
 
2.6.3. Geographical representativeness 
Since geographical scope of the study is limited to use of SFs in DONG Energy’s power plants in Denmark, at 
least the inventory data for the use and disposal phases should be based on Danish data. Regarding where the 
feedstock and fuels are sourced they might be produced in other countries, in which case the most local data 
should be applied, preferably on at least a country or region level precision. However, the future perspective of 
the study again influences the representativeness of the data because no commercial production exists and 
therefore there is no market for some of the assessed fuels through which the production location can be 
determined. If possible international forecasts of future biomass markets should be used to estimate production 
location. A good identification of the geographical scope is important to identify the mix of background data and 
marginal processes that should be used in the modelling. For products traded on a global market the mix should 
likewise reflect where in the world the supply is produced (e.g. percentage distribution of supplier 
regions/countries).  
 
 
==PAGE:48==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
14 
 
3. 
Life cycle inventories 
The inventory of each SF is based on data from literature, adjusted to fit the scope of the study when necessary, 
e.g. by choosing the relevant country electricity mix from the Ecoinvent 3.0 database. The inventories including 
key figures and assumptions are presented for the shortlisted SFs (table A1 p 5) in the following sections.  
3.1. 
Biogas 
The biogas inventory is based on Danish data exclusively, since the biogas used by DONG Energy will most 
likely be of Danish origin. The inventory fir 1 m3 biogas is shown in table A4. The feedstock mix for the biogas 
production (named Inputmix) is based on data collected from Danish biogas producers by Jacobsen et al. (2013). 
The mix consist of liquid and solid pig and cattle manure and a fraction of solid biomass waste, in this case 
maize ensilage, which is shown in table A2 along with the dry matter (DM) content of the feedstock.

Energistyrelsen (2013) reported that the nutrients from gasified manure are easier utilized by the plants than

extra mineral N-fertilizer needed to compensate for lower N-exploitation, which is around 0.81 kg.

the input mix in table A2 25.3 kg feedstock is needed to produce 1 m3 biogas with 60% methane content. In 
order to be delivered via the natural gas grid in Denmark, the biogas needs to be upgraded to 96% methane 
content to increase the heating value (Jacobsen, 2013). The upgrading is done by removing CO2 from the biogas 
as described in Schmidt (2013). The removed CO2 is modelled as the co-product liquid CO2.

Biodiesel can be made from all the types of crude oil considered in this study (Jatropha, UCO, and Algae), and is 
in all cases modelled as a transesterification process, as this is the most widely used and best documented in 
literature (appendix E). The process is essentially the same for all three types of oil and consists of esterification 
==PAGE:51==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
17 
 
with acid catalyst to reduce the amount of free fatty acids (FFAs) and avoid soap production, followed by a 
transesterification with a base catalyst to make biodiesel, which is essentially Fatty Acid Methyl Esters (FAMEs) 
(Ma et al., 1998; Romero et al., 2011). Even though the two-step transesterification is used in all cases, the 
amounts of input and output and the specific catalysts vary a bit between the processes according to the 
feedstock. Therefore three different inventories are made for the biodiesel production from each oil type.  
3.3. 
Jatropha oil and biodiesel  
As opposed to the UCO the Jatropha oil is not a waste product, but instead obtained from the Jatropha Curcas 
plant, which is grown as an energy crop. Jatropha Curcas is a tropical plant that cannot grow in Denmark, which 
is reflected in the inventory. The countries with highest Jatropha production are Philipines and Indonesia 
(Jatropha book, 2014), However the LCA studies in literature made of Jatropha oil are for the most part based on 
India conditions. Therefore two scenarios are made for the Jatropha oil; one based on Indian energy mixes and 
rainfall, and one based on Indonesian energy mixes and rainfall. The Philippine scenario is not included, since 
the Ecoinvent 3.0 database does not include country specific data for the Philippines.  
The production of crude Jatropha oil includes the cultivation and oil extraction process. The cultivation process 
based in India requires some irrigation, since the annual rainfall is around 1100 mm (Worldbank, 2015), which is 
not enough to meet the water requirement of the Jatropha, which should be above 1200 mm at the modelled seed 
yield (Kee Lam et al., 2009; Achten et al., 2008). The Jatropha plant is known as drought resistant and can grow 
at much lower water inputs, however this will significantly affect the seed-, and thereby oil-yield (Achten, 2008). 
When grown in the Philippine and Indonesian climate no irrigation is needed, since the annual rainfall in these 
countries is 2350 and 2700 mm, respectively (Worldbank, 2015).  
The inventory of Jatropha oil is based mostly on Whitaker and Heath (2009), since this study has been cited and 
data reused in many of the other available studies, such as Ayajebi et al. (2013), and is supported by numbers 
from various other articles such as Achten et al. (2008), Ong et al. (2911),  and Kumar et al. (2012). In the 
inventory a plantation with mature trees is assumed. The complete inventory for producing 1 kg Jatropha oil is 
shown in table A8.

In the extraction process the oil content of the Jatropha seeds is assumed to be 35% and the extraction efficiency 
with solvent extraction to be 91% (Achten et al., 2008; Ajayebi et al., 2013; Whitaker and Heat, 2009; Kumar et 
al.,2012). Hexane is used as solvent, which is reported by Sayyar (2009) to give the highest efficiency. The seed 
cake left after oil extraction is assumed to be used as fertilizer on the field after oil extraction, assuming that 1 kg 
seed cake is equivalent to 0.15 kg mineral NPK (40:20:10) fertilizer (Whitaker and Heat, 2009).  
As with the UCO the Jatropha oil can be used directly at the CHP or it can be used to produce biodiesel. The 
inventory for producing Jatropha biodiesel is also the two-step transesterification process, for which the 
inventory is shown in table A9.

3.4. 
Algae oil and biodiesel 
Oil derived from cultivated algae is defined as third generation biofuel and is not yet as far in commercialization 
as either UCO or Jatropha, and therefore the literature gives mainly lab scale data, which entails uncertainties 
when extrapolated to large-scale production. It has for example been reported that upscaling the algae production 
result in yield reduction because the cells are exposed to hostile environment (Bahadar and Khan, 2013). 
However, a long row of companies are working to overcome this and other problems and commercialize algae-
based biofuels (Bahadar and Khan, 2013), and it is considered a promising feedstock for biofuels in the future. 
==PAGE:53==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
19 
 
Two overall categories of algae cultivation systems exists; Raceway ponds and closed Photo bioreactor systems 
(PBR). The PBRs has potential for higher yields, no evaporation or emissions, and no contamination 
(Stephenson, 2010). However in a comparable LCA of biodiesel from algae in raceway ponds and PBRs by 
Stephenson et al (2010), it was concluded that while algae grown in raceway ponds had a positive climate effect 
compared to fossil alternatives, the algae grown in PBRs caused a 370% increase in GWP compared to the fossil 
alternatives. Therefore this study will take offset in the raceway ponds.  
 The Algae inventory includes the cultivation, harvesting, flocculation, lysis, oil extraction, and biodiesel 
(transesterification) processes. The Algae have potential to be grown and processed anywhere, and due to the 
relatively high technology demands, the processes are assumed to take place in Denmark.  The inventory for 
producing one kg of algal oil is shown in table A10. The primary data source used for the algal oil inventory is 
Stephenson (2010), which is based on UK climate and precipitation and thereby water demand for the process 
(following evaporation and rainfall), which is assumed to be a good proxy for Danish conditions. The CO2 is 
considered to come from the atmosphere, and not directly from power plant flue gas. Due to great variations in 
inventory data from different sources it was chosen to use only Stephenson (2010) for the entire inventory.

3.5. 
Pyrolysis oil  
Pyrolysis oil can be made from any lignocellulosic material, however the most considered feedstock in literature 
is wood, and in particular fast-growing wood such as willow or poplar. This inventory is based on pyrolysis oil 
from Swedish wood chips. The process for Swedish wood chip production is taken directly from the Ecoinvent 
3.0 database as seen in the inventory in table A12, and hence the production processes are not shown here.  
The Pyrolysis oil is assumed to be produced from Swedish wood chips with 50% moisture (from Ecoinvent 3.0 
database).  Prior to pyrolysis the wood chips are pre-treated by drying them to around 10% moisture content and 
grind them into fine particles <6mm (Irribarren et al., 2012). After pre-treatment the wood particles are 
pyrolysed in a fluidized bed pyrolyser at 500 C by heating it with sand (Irribarren et al., 2012; Steele et al., 2012; 
Fan et al., 2011). Besides the pyrolysis oil the process also yields bio char and syngas, which are both burned to 
provide heat for the pyrolysis process (Steele et al., 2012; Fan et al., 2011). Hence there are no net outputs of 
these materials from the process.  Hence there are no net outputs of these materials from the process.

3.6. 
Combustion processes 
The inventories for the combustion processes for the various fuels at power plants are all based on the fossil fuel 
combustion process from the Ecoinvent 3.0 database. The specific processes used were “Heat, district or 
industrial, natural gas {Europe without Switzerland}| heat production, natural gas, at industrial furnace low-
NOx >100kW | Conseq, U” for biogas and “Heat, district or industrial, other than natural gas {Europe without 
Switzerland}| heat production, heavy fuel oil, at industrial furnace 1MW | Conseq, U” for the oil alternatives, 
which were changed to fit the bio-based SFs. First the processes were scaled to 1 MJ input energy of the fuels, 
according to the calorific values. Second the electricity production efficiencies for the specific power plants were 
used and set as a co-product, since the main function of the SFs is to start up the boilers. Third the emissions 
were adjusted according to findings from literature as seen in table A13 under the assumption that the emissions 
were related to amount of energy input, not amount of produced electricity. Also all emissions connected 
specifically to fossil fuels such as heavy metals were removed for both the gas and oil alternatives. Hence it was 
assumed that there was one combustion process for all the crude oils, and one for all the biodiesels, while the 
other fuel types had separate combustion processes. The crude oil process was also used as proxy for the 
pyrolysis oil since no sources could be found for that specifically.

4. 
Life cycle impact assessment 
The impact assessment is carried out on three levels; fuel level, plant level, and company level, each with a 
different purpose. At fuel level the fuels are assessed individually since this is a cradle-to-gate assessment where 
the combustion process (i.e. the use-phase) is not included, and therefore the fuels do not contribute with the 
same service and cannot be compared. The purpose with the fuel level assessment is to make a hotspot 
assessment for each fuel to determine where in the life cycle and which processes contributes most to the 
impacts. This detailed assessment can be used to identify processes or feedstock to look for when procuring the 
fuels, and whether any types of production methods, feedstock etc. should be avoided due to large environmental 
impacts. 
The plant level assessment is a cradle-to-grave including the use-phase as combustion of 1 MJ fuel input energy, 
making the fuels directly comparable at this level. At the plant level different power plants can also be compared 
by comparing the impacts of burning 1 MJ of the same fuel at the different plants. This comparison is useful to 
determine which power plant technology has the best environmental performance.  At company level the overall 
Functional Unit is assessed i.e. the total SF consumption (gas/oil) of Dong Energy in one year. This shows which 
plants contributes most to the overall company impact and give an indication of the yearly potential savings from 
changing to an alternative SF. The normalization and weighting is used to calculate single scores at each 
assessment level to make interpretation of the results easier. However, the characterised scores are calculated as 
well to show potential trade-offs.  
4.1. 
Biogas and natural gas 
The power plants currently using natural gas as SF are HEV and AVV. HEV uses 197 TJ input energy per year, 
and AVV2 uses 1321 TJ. Despite the low efficiencies of heat and power production (12-14%) during start-
up/shut-down, this together corresponds to around 12,600 Danish 3-person household’s annual electricity 
consumption, corresponding to 37,800 people (DONG Energy, 2015).  
4.1.1. Fuel level 
The midpoint impact scores are calculated for 1 m3 natural gas (NG) and 1 m3 biogas (BG) respectively, 
however these cannot be directly compared since the burning of the fuels are not included at this level. The 
impact scores include all production steps of the two fuels up until they are ready to use at a power plant, and is 
thus a cradle to gate assessment.  The purpose of this assessment is to identify the production processes of each 
fuel that is contributing most to the overall environmental performance without taking into account any power 
plant efficiencies. It should be kept in mind that the combustion process is thus not included at fuel level.   
To understand what is contributing to the impacts of each fuel the percentage contribution from each production 
phase are shown in graph A1 for natural gas and A2 for biogas. Especially for the natural gas there is a recurring 
pattern where the offshore extraction process of the natural gas in Norway is the main contributor to all the 
impact categories, followed by the transportation processes (i.e. energy for pressurising the natural gas grid and 
the construction of it).

For the biogas the pattern is not quite as uniform, but still the anaerobic digestion is dominating the positive 
impact of most impact categories due to emissions of N2O, CO2, CH4, NH3, and H2S, whereas the upgrading 
process and avoided manure storage contribute mostly negative. The negative impacts from the upgrading 
process of biogas can be traced back to the crediting of liquid CO2 production, based on the assumption that the 
CO2 removed from the biogas can be sold on the market. The only impact category for which this results in a 
positive impact is the ionising radiation. This is because the global market process for liquid CO2 is used for the 
crediting, which includes the use of heat from nuclear power in Ukraine. Hence the credit for liquid CO2 means 
it is not produced by consuming radioactive uranium, contributing to a positive radiation impact.  
The marine eutrophication impact (based on N) from the biogas is dominated by the inputmix process due to the 
impact from maize silage cropping even though it only constitutes 10% of the inputmix. Hence if an agricultural 
waste product could be used instead, a large amount of nutrient release would be avoided. The climate change 
impact from the biogas is mainly caused by the anaerobic digestion due to the emission of GHGs (N2O, CO2 
and CH4) from the digestion plant. This effect is somehow counteracted by the avoided manure storage due to 
avoidance of emissions of the same compounds.  
The above characterisation scores show which processes contributes to which impacts; however they do not 
show the relative size between the impacts. Normalisation of the impact scores make them comparable under the 
assumption that all categories are equally important, i.e. they are all weighted the same with a weighting factor 
of 1. Graph A3 shows the normalised impact scores for natural gas and biogas.  
 
Graph A 3: Normalised impact scores of biogas and natural gas excluding long-term effects. 
The normalised scores are shown without the long-term effects, which primarily affects the toxicity impacts and 
freshwater eutrophication. The affects are caused primarily by metals and other non-degradable substances 
leaking over thousands of years e.g. from landfills. When doing the assessment the long-terms affects are

The toxicity impacts are still important, but not so much more than the others as it may seem from the graph.  
For natural gas it is the fossil depletion and marine ecotoxicity that are dominating the picture; the fossil 
depletion because natural gas is a limited, fossil resource and the marine ecotoxicity primarily because of the 
chemicals used at the offshore extraction process. The metal depletion, freshwater ecotoxicity and terrestrial 
acidification can also be traced back to the offshore extraction whereas the climate change is caused by the 
transportation of the gas and leakage from pipes.  
The biogas impacts with the largest positive values are the marine eutrophication, climate change, and fossil 
depletion due to the maize cropping and anaerobic digestion as described above. The negative toxicity and metal 
depletion impacts are caused by the crediting of liquid CO2 production, but the scores are likely to be 
overestimated due to the incomplete normalisation reference, and therefore might not be as negative as shown. 
When including the long-term emissions the toxicity scores actually become positive due to the landfill 
emissions from scrapping the anaerobic digestion plant itself.  
Even though the results are shown in the same graph they still cannot be compared directly, which is for example 
apparent form climate change impact, which is much larger for the biogas prior to combustion. Even though the 
normalization shows the relative size of the impacts the weighting step is needed to assess which impacts are 
more important, and how they affect the three areas of protection (AOP); human health, ecosystems and 
resources. The weighted results for the three AOPs are shown in graph A4. When added these three weighted 
impact scores give the single score for each fuel.  
 
Graph A 4: Weighted endpoint impact scores for biogas and natural gas. 
The human health impact dominates the biogas single score due to the anaerobic digestion process and the 
climate change it causes. The ecosystems impact is also primarily dominated by this process and secondarily by 
the maize production, which is however counteracted by the liquid CO2 production credit in the upgrading 
process. For the natural gas the single score is dominated by the impact on resources, reflection the fossil 
depletion seen in the midpoint assessment, whereas the other AOP are affected very little in comparison.

4.1.2. Plant level – Midpoint 
The plant level assessment includes the use phase, i.e. the burning of the fuels, and therefore takes into account 
impacts from combustion and the energy content of the fuels as opposed to the fuel level assessment. Thus at the 
plant level the two fuels can be compared directly, since they provide the same service of 1 MJ input energy. 
Also the plants can be compared to each other when burning 1 MJ of the same fuel, as it is shown in table A13 
for natural gas. The red marking colour in the tables indicates larger impacts, where the green indicates lower 
impacts. Hence overall it is seen that HEV has lower environmental impacts than AVV2 for most categories.

In order to also see the relative size of the different impacts, graph A5 shows the normalised impact scores 1 MJ 
biogas and natural gas burned on both HEV and AVV2. The normalisation means that the impacts can be 
compared on the same scale, but when doing this an equal weighting factor of 1 is implicitly assumed.  
 
Graph A 5: normalised impact scores for burning 1 MJ biogas and natural gas at both HEV and AVV2. 
I general more categories are negative at the plant level both for the biogas and the natural gas, which is caused 
by the crediting for electricity and heat when burning the fuels. This modelling approach means that the results 
should only be used for comparison of the fuels and not as a statement of the life cycle impacts. The reason for 
the generally lower impacts results for HEV than AVV2 is that 0.121 MJ heat is credited as co-product for HEV, 
whereas only electricity is credited for AVV2. The specific process giving rise to most of the negative values is 
that of avoided municipal waste incineration to produce heat. Thus the overall energy recovery efficiency (heat + 
power) is about double for HEV compared to AVV2 and also waste incineration make up a larger part of the 
average Danish heat mix than electricity mix. Based in these environmental profiles it thus proves beneficial to 
have the hardware (a by-pass mechanism) to produce heat when the steam pressure is not high enough to drive 
the steam turbine and produce electricity and heat by reverse pressure.    
The endpoint results in graph A6 reveal that the ecotoxicity and eutrophication are not weighted very high and 
the ecosystems impact is therefore very small. The majority of the ecosystem impact is caused by climate change 
impact, which is positive for the natural gas and negative for the biogas. However, the anaerobic digestion 
emissions counteract this negative effect, making the overall ecosystem impacts slightly positive for biogas.

The human health impact is likewise dominated by the climate change impact, and further particulate matter 
formation is much more negative for the biogas than the natural gas due to avoided manure storage, making the 
human health impact dominating for the biogas. For the natural gas the resources are again the largest 
contributor to the overall impact due to the fossil depletion.  
All in all the biogas single-score is negative and hence it performs better than the natural gas for which the single 
score is positive. HEV has a lower impact than AVV2 of approximately 5% for natural gas and 10% for biogas. 
These endpoint results confirm the conclusions that biogas is environmentally preferable over natural gas, and 
that the heat production, i.e. the steam turbine by-pass function at HEV, has environmental benefits compared to 
only co-producing electricity even though the electricity co-production is slightly decreased by this.

4.1.3. Company level  
On company level the functional unit of is assessed, i.e. the sum of SF burned per year on all plants in DONG 
Energy. This adds a factor related to how much fuels is used on each plant relative to each other, and also sheds 
light on the total environmental impact of using either fuel compared to just 1 MJ as on plant level. In table A15 
the characterised impact scores are given with the colours indicating whether natural gas or biogas performs 
better environmentally on the specific impact category. None of the fuels performs unequivocally better than the 
other, however the biogas performs best in 12 of the 18 categories.

When normalising the results the relative size of the impacts can be seen as in graph A7. The difference in 
marine eutrophication (Based on N emissions) is caused by production of maize silage for the biogas inputmix, 
where N is used as fertilizer. The freshwater eutrophication is calculated based on P emissions and therefore this 
impact is affected to a lesser extent, but still enough for the natural gas to perform better in this category as well.  
The marine ecotoxicity and fossil depletion impact from the natural gas are both very large compared to those of 
the biogas. This is caused by the use of natural gas itself and energy and chemicals for the extraction process 
offshore. The toxicity impacts are negative for the biogas due to the credit for liquid CO2 production, whereas 
the terrestrial acidification and particulate matter formation are negative due to the avoided manure storage.   
At company level equal amounts of biogas and natural gas in terms of input energy are burned at the CHPs, and 
therefore equal amounts of electricity and heat are credited for each system. While this results in negative 
impacts for biogas in most categories, it only just levels out most of the impacts from natural gas, hence many of 
the impact scores range close to 0.

on all three parameters than natural gas.

4.2. 
Oil alternatives 
The power plants currently using natural gas as SF are ASV2, ASV5, ESV, SSV, AVV1. IN total the plants use 
423 TJ input energy per year. 
4.2.1. Fuel level  
The SF alternatives for HFO are assessed in term of hotspots and which processes contributes to which impacts.  
HFO 
The characterisation impact results for HFO (Heavy fuel oil) which is the most commonly used SF, are shown in 
graph A9, where the percentage contribution of each sub-process can be seen. It is clear that the extraction 
process and oil refinery itself is the most contributing process for all impact categories. This large influence is 
caused by the energy and chemicals used in the extraction and refining processes.  
 
Graph A 9: Characterisation impact scores for production of 1 kg HFO. Percentage distribution of contributing processes. 
The next most important process for the HFO is the transportation processes after refining, since the HFO is 
traded on a global market and therefore is transported long distances to e.g. Denmark. The electricity used for 
scrubbing and during storage constitutes 12% in the freshwater eutrophication impact.  All other processes give 
rise to less than 2% of the total impact in all categories, and are therefore not considered to be significant. 
When looking at the normalised impact scores in graph A10 and assuming equal weighting, the fossil depletion 
and freshwater and marine ecotoxicity impacts are the largest. The fossil depletion is from using the oil itself 
whereas the ecotoxicity is water discharge from the extraction process that is polluted with oil. The same water 
discharge is also the cause of the human toxicity from the oil extraction and refinery process.

When assessing the HFO weighted endpoint impacts in graph A11 it is clear that the oil extraction and refinery 
is still the dominating process for all three areas of protection, and that the resource impact driven mostly by the 
fossil depletion is more than ten times larger than the human health and ecosystem impacts put together. The 
fossil depletion is an inherent characteristic for fossil fuels and the only method for reducing this impact is 
simply not using the fuel.

UCO and UCO biodiesel 
The UCO (used cooking oil) is a waste product, and therefore there are no impacts associated with producing it 
other than the purification, where water and organic particles from the food that was cooked in the oil is 
removed. Included in the purification process is the collection of the oil from restaurants, called transport.  
For the biodiesel the transesterification process comes directly after the purification. As seen from the 
characterisation scores in graph A12 the Transesterification dominates most of the processes except the 
photochemical oxidant formation. The impacts can for the most part be traced back to the crediting for glycerine 
production, which is originally produced from waste products of other processes, and therefore the crediting 
gives positive rather than negative results.  
 
Graph A 12: Characterised midpoint scores for UCO biodiesel. Percentage distribution of contributing processes 
For the purification process most of the impacts can be traced back to the electricity used, which is modelled as 
the Danish electricity mix and hence depends highly on coal. However the negative impacts from the 
purification process is associated with waste incineration of the organic material removed from the oil, which is 
used for energy production. The transportation of the used cooking oil constitutes a large part of the land use 
impact and the metal depletion impact due to the extraction of diesel to fuel the trucks. However, when looking 
at the normalised scores in graph A13 and assuming an equal weighting, the impact categories where 
transportation constituted a significant part are all very small (Land use, metal depletion, photochemical oxidant 
formation).

impacts as well as for most of the others, the transesterification process is still dominating.  
When looking at the endpoint characterisation of the UCO biodiesel process in graph A14 it looks very similar to 
the midpoint characterisation with transesterification dominating the picture and resulting in positive values 
except for the climate change, and fossil depletion.

crediting for glycerine production, but because it is normally produced from fossil resources the negative impact 
scores of not using these are determining for the final outcome.  
Jatropha oil and biodiesel 
The Jatropha characterisation impact scores are seen in graph A16, and even though the transesterification 
process is roughly the same as for the UCO biodiesel, it is the cultivation process that is dominating most of the 
impact categories. The negative impact form the cultivation is caused by crediting for electricity production. The 
electricity is generated from burning seed husk and replaces the Indonesian electricity mix, which is primarily 
based on coal. However, since fertiliser, pesticides and land are used in the cultivation process it also affects 
impacts such as land use, toxicity and eutrophication positively.  
The oil extraction process is credited for fertiliser from the seed cake, which gives rise to the negative impacts in 
toxicity and eutrophication categories. Also the water and metal depletion impacts are negative because these 
resources are normally used in fertiliser production. The transesterification only gives rise to positive impacts 
due to the energy consumption, and the glycerine credited.

because it gives negative impact in these categories.

as well terrestrial acidification and particulate matter formation impacts are negetive.

When applying the weighting factors and looking at the endpoint assessment (graph A18) the importance of the 
cultivation process is underlined; both in terms of the negative impacts in human health and resources from 
electricity crediting, and the positive impact in ecosystems from the use of fertiliser and land. Even though the 
transesterification process is not dominating, there still seems to be a significant difference between the Jatropha 
oil and the Jatropha biodiesel, especially in the resources and human health impacts where transesterification 
contributes quite significantly.  
 
Graph A 18: Weighted endpoint scores for Jatropha biodiesel. 
Despite the positive impacts from especially the transesterification process, the cultivation process still has the 
largest influence on the single value, which ends up being negative (-109 mPt). These results show that the 
electricity production from seed husk is important for the environmental performance of the Jatropha oil and 
Jatropha biodiesel, and much more so than the use of seed cake as fertiliser. However, it also depends on the 
electricity that is credited and these large negative impact scores are only seen because the Indonesian electricity 
mix that is replaces is based mainly on coal and other fossil fuels.  
Algal oil and biodiesel 
The midpoint characterisation scores of the algal based fuel in graph A19 shows that the cultivation and 
dewatering (flocculation) of the algal oil, as well as the transesterification for the algal biodiesel, all contributes 
to the positive side of the impacts scores. The only exception is the water depletion, since the water removed 
from the concentrated harvested algae is recycled back into the system. The oil extraction process on the other 
hand contributes to the negative side of the impacts for all categories except for the water depletion because the 
electricity used causes water depletion. These negative impact scores for the oil extraction is caused by the 
crediting of energy produced from the residues after oil extraction, which are assumed to be used as feedstock 
for anaerobic digestion, hence producing biogas. It should be noted though that these residues, which consist 
mainly of proteins and hydrocarbons might have alternative uses for energy or animal feed purposes, and this is 
just one possible scenario of their use.

Graph A 19: midpoint characterisation scores of algal biodiesel. Percentage distribution of contributing processes 
As for the other biodiesel fuels the transesterification is positive mainly due to the glycerine produced as co-
product, which causes waste products from other processes not to be used, and to a smaller extent due to the 
energy consumption in the process. For the cultivation process the nitrogen fertiliser used is the main cause of all 
of the impacts, and actually the nitrogen fertiliser constitutes a larger part (70 %) of the water depletion impact 
than the tap water (29%) used for the process.  
 In the dewatering process aluminium sulphate is used for flocculation and together with the electricity 
consumption is the main cause for the impacts of the dewatering process. In the eutrophication and depletion 
impacts the electricity consumption stands for the largest part, whereas the aluminium sulphate does so for the 
toxicity impacts.   
Because all processes contributes most to different impact categories, there is no singular pattern in the 
characterisation scores of which process gives the largest contributions. However, the normalisation scores in 
graph A20 can give a better indication of this, when assuming the weighting is equal.  
As for most of the other fuels it can be seen that the marine and human toxicity impacts are the highest, and that 
the transesterification process is the main process driving these impacts, followed by the cultivation process. 
However, as for the other processes, the normalised toxicity impacts are likely overestimated, and hence the 
eutrophication, depletion and climate change impacts might be just as important. For these processes the oil 
extraction is the main driver in most, always contributing negatively to the scores, whereas the dewatering and 
cultivation are driving the positive side of the impact scores.

Pyrolysis oil  
AS opposed to the other bio-based oil alternatives the impacts of the pyrolysis oil are all positive, since no co-
products exists in any of the production processes, and therefore no credit is given. The wood used is assumed to 
be planted and harvested for the purpose of energy wood, and therefore the cultivation process is included in the 
wood chips production process (in blue) in graph A22. When looking at the characterisation scores the wood 
chips production seems to have a large influence on most of the impacts together with the pre-treatment process.   
Most of the impacts from the wood chips production come from the fossil fuel use during the life cycle for 
planting, harvesting, and chipping. In the pre-treatment process the transport of the wood chips with truck from 
the forest is the source of most impacts also due to the use of fossil fuels, except for the ionising radiation and 
marine ecotoxicity which are caused by electricity consumption.  
 
Graph A 22: Characterises impact score for pyrolysis oil. Percentage distribution of contributing processes 
When normalising the impact scores (graph A23) it is apparent that with equal weighting the marine ecotoxicity, 
human toxicity, and ionising radiation are the largest. The marine ecotoxicity can for all three processes be 
traced back to the use of fossil fuel and offshore extraction. However, due to the incomplete normalisation 
reference the ecotox impacts are likely to be overestimated even when excluding long-term effects.  
In the human toxicity impact the pre-treatment processes is also dominating, but in this case the impact is caused 
mainly by the brake wear of the trucks, and only to a lesser extent by the burning of diesel. For the pyrolysis 
process the wood ash is the cause of the human toxicity impact. The ionising radiation impact is driven by the 
pre-treatment and the pyrolysis processes and is caused by the use of electricity in Denmark, of which a large 
share is produced from coal.  
Also processes like fossil depletion, metal depletion, climate change, freshwater and terrestrial ecotoxicity as 
well as particulate and photochemical oxidant formation can be traced back to the fossil fuels used in the 
production processes for pyrolysis oil, whether it is used as electricity or burned in diesel engines of trucks or 
forest machinery.

Graph A 23: Normalised impact scores for pyrolysis oil 
When performing endpoint assessment on the pyrolysis oil (graph A24) the production of wood chips seems to

production, is primarily caused by land use and the transformation of land into intensive forest management.  
Both the resources and human health impacts are driven by the wood chip production and the pre-treatment and 
in both cases by the fossil energy used in these processes as well as the land use to a minor extent.  
These weighted endpoint results show that the feedstock used for the pyrolysis oil is determining for the overall 
environmental impact, which can thus be reduced significantly by using for instance waste product feedstock.

4.2.2. Plant level 
Dong Energy currently has five plants using HFO as SF. When burning SFs to heat up the boiler most of these 
plants produce electricity, but they do not produce the same amount of electricity or emit the same amount of 
NOx and SO2 per MJ of fuel input. To see what difference this makes each fuel is assessed on plant level, i.e. 
comparing burning 1 MJ of fuel at each of the five plants. 
Even though the fuel level assessment is useful for assessing each fuel individually and findings hotspots in the 
production processes, the fuels cannot be directly compared because the function of 1 kg fuel is not the same for 
all the fuels due to difference in energy contents. On plant level on the other hand the use-phase is added, i.e. the 
burning of the fuels at the power plants, which is modelled as burning 1 MJ of fuel. Hence the function becomes 
the same (providing 1 MJ of energy) and the impacts of producing and burning 1 MJ of each fuel can be directly 
compared.  
HFO  
Graph A25 shows the weighted impact scores for burning 1 MJ HFO at each of the five CHPs including the 
production processes described in the fuel level assessment above.  
 
Graph A 25: Weighted endpoint scores for producing and burning 1 MJ HFO at DONG Energy’s power plants 
The impacts of the CHP process are shown for the three areas of protection. As seen in the graph the resources 
impact is negative for the two boilers ASV2 and ASV5, which is caused by the crediting for electricity and 
thereby avoided use of coal. The resources impact from the HFO is includd in the production process, which is 
why this crediting becomes negative for the burning process alone. The resouces impact follows the amount of 
electricity credited, i.e. the more electricity produced, the more negative the impact. For ESV and SSV the 0,008 
kWh and 0,015 kWh of electricity credited per MJ fuel input is too little to give a negative impact and instead 
the metal depletion gives the positive impact.

4.2.3. Company level 
Comparing fuels based on functional unit with the purpose to assess which fuels perform better and would be 
best for dong to use form an environmental point of view.  
Midpoint 
As it was seen in the fuel level assessment most of the fuels have the highest impact scores in the marine and 
human toxicity categories, which is therefore also the case on company level, but it should still be kept in mind 
that these impacts are overestimated due to the incomplete normalisation reference. 
When comparing the characterised impact scores of the SFs (table A15) it is seen that HFO has the largest 
impact in 9 of the 14 impact categories if equal weighting is assumed. Algal biodiesel has larger impact than 
HFO in four categories, pyrolysis oil, algal oil and Jatropha biodiesel in three, and UCO biodiesel in two. The 
categories where HFO performs best, i.e. has lowest impact scores, compared to the other fuels is the 
eutrophication, land use, and metal depletion categories, all of which are due to fertiliser use for the bio-based 
fuels as described in the fuel level assessment.

5. 
Uncertainty and sensitivity 
5.1. 
Biogas 
In the impact assessment of biogas the production processes contributing most to the environmental impacts 
were identified. Most of these processes build on assumptions about production methods or quantity of inventory 
data form literature. Therefore a sensitivity check is made for these highly contributing processes.  
5.1.1. Maize silage for inputmix 
Even though the maize silage constitutes only 10 wt% of the inputmix, it is the main reason for most of the 
inputmix-related impacts, since it is grown directly for the purpose, whereas the manure is a waste product 
entering the process with no upstream impacts. Of the impacts where the maize silage production contributes 
significantly the land use, marine eutrophication, and terrestrial acidification are larger for the biogas than the 
natural gas after normalisation with equal weighting. To assess the system’s sensitivity to the use of maize silage 
it is investigated how these impact scores change when changing the input of maize silage.  Two scenarios are 
investigated (1) replacing the maize silage with grass silage, (2) replacing the maize silage with straw. 
Characterisation impact scores for both scenarios are shown in graph A31 with maize silage as base scenario. As 
seen in the graph there is a clear distinction between biogas made from the three inputmixes even though only 
the 10 wt% plant material in the biogas inputmix was changed. The largest percentage changes are seen in the 
categories terrestrial acidification, freshwater eutrophication, human toxicity, particulate matter formation, and 
marine ecotoxicity, where the difference is between a factor 5 and 20.   
 
Graph A 31: Characterisation impact scores for three inputmix scenarios: (1) Maize silage, (2) grass silage, (3) Straw 
-2500%
-2000%
-1500%
-1000%
-500%
0%
500%
1000%
1500%
2000%
2500%
Inputmix; Maize silage
Inputmix; Grass silage

==PAGE:83==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix A 
 
49 
 
Generally straw based inputmix performs better than maize and grass silage except in the categories ozone 
depletion, photochemical oxidant formation and land use. However, when looking at the normalised scores 
(excluding long-term) in graph A32 these categories are small compared to e.g. marine ecotoxicity, human 
toxicity, and freshwater eutrophication, where the inputmix with straw has much lower impact scores than the 
other two scenarios. It should be noted though, that if the long-term impacts are included the marine ecotoxicity 
score will be positive for all three inputmix-types, and therefore the large negative value is most likely 
overestimated due to incompleteness of the normalisation reference.  
 
Graph A 32: Normalised impact scores for the three inputmix scenarios (1) maize silage, (2) grass silage, (3) straw 
To assess the overall influence of changing the plant-based fraction of the inputmix, the weighted scores for the 
three scenarios as well as the natural gas are shown in graph A33. In this graph it can be seen that the large 
negative value for marine and human toxicity does not have as big an importance in the single score value, since 
the ecosystem and human health impacts are still overall positive, however they might have been more so 
without the large negative scores.  
Based on the impact results for the three systems compared to the natural gas it is concluded that biogas 
performs better than the natural gas in any of the three scenarios, and that this is mainly due to the high 
weighting of the fossil depletion impact, which is dominating the natural gas single score. Further it is concluded 
that the plant-based fraction of the biogas inputmix change the end results significantly with a factor of more 
than three in the single score values for the analysed scenario with highest (Grass-silage) and lowest (straw)  
impact. However, the difference is not large enough to exceed the impact of the natural gas, and therefore biogas 
should still be preferred as fuel.

Graph A 33: Weighted impact scores for burning 1 MJ natural gas vs. biogas with three different inputmixes 
5.1.2. Liquid CO2 crediting 
The crediting for liquid CO2 production in the biogas purification gives rise to significant negative values in a 
number of impact categories (graph A2). This assumption builds on a best practice assumption that the CO2 
removed from the raw biogas (with 60% CH4) is collected, treated and sold as liquid CO2. However, this 
process might not be available at all anaerobic digestion plants, and the CO2 might just be released to the 
atmosphere. Since this is an all or nothing assumption (either the CO2 is captured or it is not) the sensitivity 
scenario is to release the CO2 to the atmosphere and not credit for liquid CO2 production.  
The impact scores without the liquid CO2 crediting is shown in graph A34. As opposed to the base case the 
upgrading process now contributes positively to all impact categories, except the water depletion. The ionising 
radiation impact has on the contrary decreased considerably, since no nuclear power is now involved.

5.1.3. Manure storage 
The avoided manure storage contributes negatively to the impact scores: climate change, acidification, marine 
eutrophication, particulate matter, and photochemical oxidant formation. No other midpoint categories are 
affected by the manure storage (i.e. zero impact).  Most of the emissions from manure storage are based on N-
content of the manure, and to assess how sensitive the system is to changes in manure composition, the N 
content and thereby the emissions/kg manure is decreased 10%. Table A16 shows the inventories of the original 
and the decreased emission scenario for manure storage.

For the weighted endpoint scores at fuel level, which are seen in graph A38, the decreased manure emissions 
result in no change for the resource impact, 4% increase in the ecosystems impact, and 11% increase in the 
human health impact primarily due to climate change. Thus the single score value increases 8% in total, which is 
a small change compared to the weighted impact score for natural gas, which is more than three times as large as 
the biogas impact. The uncertainty related to manure storage is thus not enough to change the final conclusion 
than biogas has lesser impact than natural gas.

5.2. 
 Oil alternatives 
5.2.1. Effect of excluding long-term impacts 
The long-term emissions have been excluded from the assessments, because they often cause very high impact 
scores in especially ecotoxicity categories in midpoint assessments. This is illustrated by the case of UCO, where 
including and excluding long term effects in the midpoint assessment are illustrated in graph A39 under the 
assumption of equal weighting. When including the long-term effects the freshwater and marine ecotoxicity 
impacts are two orders of magnitude larger than the largest of the other impacts, and thus make all other 
categories seem insignificant. When excluding the long-term effects, the marine and ecotoxicity impacts are still 
the largest, but only by around three times larger than the others.   
 
Graph A 39 Illustration of the large effect of long-term effects on certain impact categories for normalised midpoint scores (UCO) 
This effect is only seen at the midpoint scores, whereas in the endpoint scores including and excluding of long-
term effects does not change the results significantly, neither at fuel level nor at company level. This might be 
correlated with the marine and freshwater ecotoxicities not having a large effect on the three areas of protection 
in either case. This can be illustrated by looking at the endpoint assessment at company level, where  the single 
scores does not change much, as seen in graph A40. The only impact score that shows above 10% difference 
between including and excluding long-term emissions is that of Jatropha biodiesel for which the single score 
including long-term is 11% more negative. Whether the long-term effects are included or excluded does not 
change the ranking of which fuel is preferable in the company level assessment. Thus excluding long-term 
emissions in the midpoint assessment better reflects the results from the endpoint assessment by not ascribing 
such large importance to especially the ecotoxicity processes.

5.2.2. Glycerine crediting for transesterification 
The Glycerine production from the waste products of the transesterification process stands for a large part of the 
impact of the transesterification process itself across all impact categories as seen in graph A41. The glycerine is 
a co-product from the biodiesel production and is therefore modelled as an avoided product in the consequential 
framework, but despite this the glycerine contributes positively to most of the impact categories. This is because 
the avoided glycerine production is based on allyl chloride, which has the co-product HCL that lead to negative 
impacts for allyl chloride. However if the allyl chloride is not used because glycerine is produced from the 
biodiesel process instead, the HCL production can no longer be credited, and the impacts become positive for 
avoiding glycerine production.  
 
Graph A 41: Characterised midpoint scores for the transesterification process for UCO at fuel level

The problem arises because the impacts for the crediting of HCL are more important than the production 
processes.  This is because system expansion is used systematically in the Ecoinvent database. To solve the 
problem allocation is used according to the ISO hierarchy of multi-functional processes, because system 
expansion is not possible. Allocation is made on the allyl chloride process for the production, and then re-linked 
to the consequential processes to avoid the crediting of HCL.  
As seen in graph A42 this changes results in glycerine contributing negatively to all impact categories but water 
depletion, and hence the entire impact profile for the transesterification process changes. The impacts from 
glycerine crediting have not only changed sign, but also the relative size of the impacts has changed, for instance 
in the terrestrial ecotoxicity and ionising radiation.  
 
Graph A 42: Characterised midpoint scores for the transesterification process for UCO at fuel level – new allyl chloride process 
The changes in the transesterification process imply changes in all biodiesel alternatives. The endpoint results 
before and after changing the glycerine process are shown in graph A43. Changing the glycerine process mean 
that all endpoint results are a bit more negative than they were for the original process, however the ranking of 
the fuels in terms of total environmental impact is not changed. Since the amount of produced glycerine form the 
process is roughly the same for all biodiesel types, the change is around 30 mPt for all three. However, the 
percentage change varies form 20% to 70% due to the different relative sizes of the biodiesel types. It should be 
noted that this does not change the ranking of the alternative SFs at company level either.

5.2.3. Jatropha form another country 
In the study it is assumed that Jatropha cultivation and oil extraction takes place in India, however studies were 
found where jatropha was grown in Indonesia as well, and here it is investigated if it will have different 
environmental impact to use jatropha from India compared to Indonesia. When changing from Indian to 
Indonesian production the yield and fertiliser use is expected to be the same, since these parameters are more 
dependent on the local changes and soil types than the regional changes. The parameters that do change is the 
transportation distance, the electricity mix, and the irrigation, which is needed in India but not in Indonesia due 
to higher annual rainfall (see table A18), which is above the needed 1200 mm/year (Archten, 2008).  
Table A 20: Annual rainfall and irrigation needs for jatropha production in India and Indonesia 
India 
1083 mm/year 
Irrigation needed 
Indonesia 
2702 mm/year 
No irrigation needed 
 
When assessing the differences in normalised impact scores (equal weighting) between Indian and Indonesian 
Jatropha oil (including extraction), the largest percentage changes are for the water depletion (97% decrease), 
freshwater ecotoxicity (54% increase), and ozone depletion (42% decrease). However, these impacts are smaller 
by two orders of magnitude than e.g. photochemical oxidant formation (6% increase). To assess the overall 
influence of changing cultivation country, the endpoint scores are shown in graph A44. The endpoint results 
show that the Indian grown Jatropha has lower environmental impact overall by around 2%, which is primarily 
due to minor differences in the electricity mix. Both countries use coal as primary energy source for electricity 
(around 70%), however, in Indonesia around 25% are renewables (hydro and geothermal energy) as opposed to 
India where it is only around 15%. Since the electricity mix is mostly used for crediting (i.e. electricity 
production from Jatropha residues) this means that more fossils are replaced in India than in Indonesia, hence the 
more negative impact score. However, the difference is so small (2%) that it is exceeded by uncertainties in 
inventory data, and therefore in practice there is no difference in environmental impact between   Indian and 
Indonesian grown Jatropha. The difference that might exist lies in the management of the land and the 
cultivation methods rather than country-specific data.

5.2.4. Pyrolysis feedstock 
The fuel level assessment of pyrolysis oil showed that the cultivation and harvesting of wood for feedstock was 
the determining process for the endpoint score due to the fossil fuels used in the processes. Therefore a 
sensitivity assessment is made by replacing the wood feedstock with straw instead.  
 
Graph A 45: weighted endpoint scores at fuel level of 1 kg pyrolysis oil from wood chips and straw 
As seen from  graph A45 the endpoint score is still determined very much by the feedstock used, however for the 
straw both the human health and resources impacts are negative.   
Since the endpoint single score for the straw-based pyrolysis oil is 0.77 mPt compared to 29.94 mPt for the 
wood-based pyrolysis oil, or a factor 39 smaller, it is considered significant. This can also be seen on graph A46, 
which shows the endpoint scores of all fuels at company level including the straw pyrolysis oil. When the 
combustion process is added, the endpoint is not so different from the wood based pyrolysis oil, because the 
electricity credited has greater impacts for both feedstock. At company level the endpoint single score is 0.51 
MPt for the straw-based pyrolysis oil and 1.31 MPt for the wood-based, hence only a factor 2.6 smaller. Even 
though this change might still be significant, it is not enough to change the ranking of the fuels in terms of total

5.2.5. Algae 
The algae production for energy or other purposes is an emerging technology, and hence no data exist in the 
Ecoinvent database, and the data found in literature varies significantly. The report from Stephenson et al. (2010) 
was used as source for the assessment, since the algae was grown in UK, which was the study with closest 
resemblance to Danish climate and technology development. Especially the first is important since the algae are 
grown in open raceway ponds and evaporation and precipitation are therefore important for the amount of water 
needed. Also the life cycle inventory data found in Stephenson et al (2010) was more comprehensive and in the 
same order of magnitude as other sources (Kumar et al., 2012; Bahadar and Kahn, 2013). 
Another source that was considered was the article from Ajayebi et al (2013), which considered an Indian case. 
The extraction process of this study builds on the one from Stephenson et al (2013), but the other life cycle 
stages are based on other sources. In graph A47 the characterised impact scores are shown for both Stephenson 
et al (2010) and Ajayebi et al (2013). The inventory data from Ajayebi et al (2013) causes higher impacts in all 
categories, which is primarily due to the higher fertiliser consumption; 0.88 kg N and 0.74 kg P per kg biodiesel 
versus 0.059 kg N I Stephenson et al (2013), which is based on restricted N growth for higher lipid production in 
the algae. Also the electricity crediting is higher in Stephenson (4.36 kWh) than Ajayebi (3.99 kWh), and the 
extraction efficiency (kg oil/kg dry algae) is higher for Stephenson (36%) than Ajayebi (23%). In the endpoint 
single score the Ajayebi inventory (781 mPt) has almost ten times the impact of Stephenson (78.4 mPt).  
At company level when the combustion at power plants is added, the perfromance of algal oil and algal biodiesel 
based on inventories form Ajayebi is even larger compared to thealgal fuels based on inventories from 
Stephenson et al (2010) with a factor of around 60 (see graph A 48). This result exceeds all other assessed fuels 
and the impact is almost 16 times that of HFO which had the largest impacts of the other fuels. This indicates 
that there are some biases or differences in assumptions between this study and the one made by Ajayebi et al. 
(2013), and inventory from Stephensin et al. (2010), which yeilds results in the same order of magnistude as the 
other fuels is therefore used. Also the article by Ajayebi et al. (2013) was found to have some typo’s in the data 
tables, and even after contacting the authors some of the numbers still did not add up.
