This appendix gives and overview of the boiler types at DONG Energy’s power plants and their characteristics. 
Also a description is given of support fuels and how they are used, and lastly the support fuel use and demand in 
DONG Energy is described based on 2014 data. (All illustrations are provided by DONG Energy). 
==PAGE:99==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix B 
 
2 
 
B.1. Boiler types in DONG Energy  
All the central power plants in DONG Energy are Combined Heat and Power plants (CHP plants), producing 
both electricity and heat. The principle of how the boiler works is depicted in Figure B.1 below. The fuel (in this 
case coal) is fired into the boiler to produce water vapour, which drives a turbine to produce electricity, and is 
then condensed in a heat exchanger to supply heat to the district heating network. By the help of cooling water 
(sea water in the DONG Energy plants), the water is then cooled further and then pumped into the cycle again.  
 
Figure B.1: Illustration of the heat and power creation processes in A CHP plant 
Dong Energy has five overall boiler types in their portfolio; dust boiler, grate boiler, straw boiler, oil boiler and 
gas boiler. Each boiler type differs in the hardware and connected feeding system. The straw boiler is 
characterized by a straw-ripper, which is designed to rip entire bales of straw apart into 5 cm pieces, before they 
are fed into the boiler (Figure B 2a) with high pressure air. This mechanism is found on SSV and HEV 
(Studstrup and Herning power plants). Grate boilers burn solid fuels that are not ground prior to feeding, but are 
fed directly onto an inclined grate in the bottom of the boiler, increasing the retention time sufficiently for the 
coarse fuels. Grate boilers are used for wood chips, and also at AVV (Avedøre power plant) whole bales of straw 
are burned on a grate without prior cutting. Dust boilers are used for solid fuels that are pulverised in a mill 
(Figure B 2b) before they are blown into the boiler as dust using high pressure air (Yan and LU, 2014).  
==PAGE:100==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix B 
 
3 
 
 
Figure B 2a: Straw-ripper   
                                        Figure B 2b: Mill used for coal and wood pellets 
The feeding mechanism of a dust boiler, called a dust burner, can be seen in Figure B 3a and b. As seen in Figure 
B 3b the dust is fired in with the primary air (the green area), which corresponds to the second circle from the 
inside on Figure B 3a. The two outer circles feed secondary and tertiary air, which is used when a larger load, i.e. 
a larger flame, is needed. To increase the load the concentration of dust in the primary air is increased and 
sufficient secondary and/or tertiary air is provided to ensure full burnout of the dust and maintain the flame 
shape. In the middle of the dust burner is the oil feeding pipe (white area in the middle), through which the HFO 
(Heavy Fuel Oil), which is used as SF (support fuel) is fed into the boiler.   
 
Figure B 3a: Dust burner in boiler wall 
                          Figure AB 3b: Cross section of a dust burner with oil as SF 
 
 
==PAGE:101==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix B 
 
4 
 
B.2. Dust fired boilers and support fuel use 
The SFs are needed specifically in the dust boilers due to the special feeding mechanism, where the dust fuel is 
blown into the boiler with air. This differs from grate boilers, where the fuel is lying on a grate in the bottom of 
the boiler, and from the oil and gas burners, since these fuels are fed in without air. A coal flame requires a 
certain intensity to keep burning, otherwise it will be unstable and the boiler will shut down. Further, the primary 
air (with the coal/wood pellet dust) needs to enter the boiler with a certain velocity, vmin, higher than the flame's 
propagation velocity, to prevent back-burning into the burner feeding mechanism. Also the cloud of dust needs 
to have a certain concentration, Cmin, for the flame to be sustained. The two together (vmin and Cmin) determines 
the minimum flow of dust fuel, Qmin (mass per time), that needs to be fired into the boiler, and subsequently the 
minimum load at which the boiler can run on coal/wood pellets. This means that the boiler needs to obtain a high 
temperature and therefore run above the minimum load, in order to sustain the flame.  However, there are 
situations when it is necessary to operate the boiler at a lower load, for instance when shutting down or starting 
up the boiler due to various reasons such as maintenance, low power/heat demand, tests etc. In these situations 
the SF is used. In DONG Energy the fuels used as SFs are either natural gas form the Danish gas grid or HFO 
(heavy fuel oil), depending on the configuration of the plant. Only one type of SF is used at each plant (Alstom, 
2014). 
B.2.1. Start-up of boilers  
When starting and shutting down the boiler it cannot be done directly on coal, i.e. going from zero to minimum 
coal load in one step, since this would result in too high temperature changes and thereby temperature gradients 
in the hardware parts , which would cause unnecessary tearing and metal fatigue. Therefore the SFs are used, 
which can be controlled more precisely and hence fired in more gradually within the low load zone. The SFs are 
characterised by lower flash points compared to the primary fuels (Table B 1), and due to the liquid/gaseous 
form of the SFs they can be fed in without air, hence preventing back-fire into the feeding mechanism, which 
means that a much lower minimum flow (Qmin) can be obtained. These characteristics render firing at low loads 
possible. SFs are fed into the boiler through a pipe in the middle of dust burner, which makes it possible to start 
the boiler by igniting the SF into a small flame and then start firing in the dust fuel around it, which is then 
ignited by the SF flame, to generate a larger flame that can support itself, and the SF can be removed.  
Table B 1: Flash point of the primary dust fuels coal and wood pellets and of the SFs  
(sources: 1: Flash points – fuels (2014) / 2: Praxair (2009) / 3: ABS (1984) / 4: Fuels and Chemicals – Autoignition Temperatures 
(2014) / 5: Lukoil (2015)) 
 
Coal 
Wood pellets 
(pine/oak) 
Natural gas 
Heavy fuel oil 
Flash point 
70-120 C1 
N/A 
-188 C2 
60 C minimum3 
Auto ignition 
temperature 
349 C4 
427 C4 / 482 C4 
580 C –600 C2 
220-550 C5 
 
The flash point of a compound is the lowest temperature at which it will generate sufficient vapour to flash, 
when exposed to a source of ignition, i.e. a spark (ABS, 1984). The autoignition temperature on the other hand is 
the temperature at which the compound will flare and continue burning without any ignition source. The latter is 
the temperature in the boiler needs to reach to start up new galleries without using SF. The fuel oil and natural 
gas are both ignited by a spark from an ignition mechanism, and hence can be used at lower boiler temperatures.  
 
==PAGE:102==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix B 
 
5 
 
The burners can be positioned in two different ways within the boiler; First option is burners placed in rows at 
opposite sides of the boiler wall with a similar row on the wall across. This is seen in Figure B 4b. The other 
option is to position four burners in each gallery, one in each corner of the boiler. Each of the burners feed the 
fuel, and hence the flame, in an angle towards one side, to create a rotating circular motion of the flame, called a 
fireball boiler. When seen from above this looks like the flame in Figure B 4a. DONG Energy has both types of 
boilers in their portfolio, but the burner configuration does not affect which type of primary and support fuel that 
can be used in the boiler.  
 
 
Figure B 4a: Fireball boiler from above 
Figure B 4b: Four burners in a row in the boiler wall.  
(Source: Morris, 2011) 
 
 
(Source: Yan and Lu, 2014) 
The boiler contains multiple burners in various heights along the boiler wall (as the four in a row seen in Figure 
B 4b), making up galleries, which thus consist of a collection of boilers in the same height. As an example the 
boiler at Esbjerg Værket (ESV) has 6 galleries, with a total of 24 coal burners (4 on each gallery) but only 20 of 
them are connected to oil burners (4 on each of 5 of the galleries). The five galleries with the oil burners are 
shown diagrammatically in Figure B 5. The diagram shows a cold start-up of boiler 3 at ESV (ESV3), and how 
the SF, in this case HFO, is used. 
 
==PAGE:103==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix B 
 
6 
 
 
Figure B 5: Diagram showing the cold start-up of the ESV3 boiler. (Source: Alstom, 2014) 
 
The cold start-up in this example starts with oil burners 1-1 and 1-3 in the first gallery, 1st Level, being ignited 
followed by oil burners 1-2 and 1-4. When they are running the oil burners 2-1 to 2-4 on the neighbour gallery 
are ignited, and soon after the first coal burners, C1-10 can be started. More oil burners (3rd Level) are started, 
and then the associated coal burners. When the oil burners in the third gallery are turned on, the ones in the first 
gallery are turned off to ensure a slow warm-up of the boiler. They are then used again when the coal burners in 
level 1st are needed. The coal burners in 4th level are started without use of oil, which is due to the interaction and 
energy exchange between the flames in the burner, especially from the neighbouring galleries, that makes it 
possible to ignite the coal flame directly. When the boiler is running on coal (i.e. above minimum load), but full 
load (100%) is not required, not all galleries are active at the same time, but are turned on and off in turns in a 
circular pattern. For this process SF is not needed, since this is a case where the boiler temperature is high 
enough to ignite the dust fuel due to the energy form the other flames.   
 
 
==PAGE:104==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix B 
 
7 
 
B.3. Support fuel use in DONG Energy 
DONG Energy Thermal Power has nine central CHP plants in their portfolio, which are listed in  
 
Table B2. Due to the different configurations of DONG Energy’s power plants, different types of SFs are used. 
Two of the boilers (AVV2 and HEV1) use natural gas, and as seen in Fejl! Henvisningskilde ikke fundet.  
these two boilers also uses the highest amounts and percentages of SF. This is because the gas is not only used as 
a SF in the traditional sense, but also as direct supplement when a higher load is required than what can be 
achieved with the primary fuel alone.  As seen in  
 
Table B both HEV1 and AVV2 use biomass, including wood pellets, as primary fuel, and the reason why the 
boilers cannot work on full load with the wood pellets alone, is that the bioconversions are not yet fully 
implemented. This means that in AVV1, only three of the four mills grinding the wood pellets were converted in 
2014 when the data was collected, but when the last mill was installed in 2015, the boiler could run full load on 
wood pellets, and hence the gas consumption is expected to decline significantly in 2015.

As seen from Figure B and B7 there is no common pattern for the plants of when the SFs are used during the 
year. For the primary fuel consumption in DONG Energy’s plants, there is usually a clear correlation which 
shows much higher fuel consumption in the cold months and very low or zero primary fuel consumption during 
the warmest summer months. This is not the case for the SF consumption, which is controlled by other 
parameters than the heat demand, and therefore peaks different times a year for the different plants. Hence, there 
is no common denominator determining the consumption of SF on the different plants, but rather it is related to 
plant-specific conditions and incidents.

This appendix gives a short overview of what biofuels are and which different types exists, as well as a 
description of the long-listed bio-based support fuels. 
==PAGE:108==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix C 
 
2 
 
C.1. Biofuel types and classification 
According to the European directive 2003/56/EC (European Union, 2003), the definition of renewable energy 
sources is “renewable non-fossil energy sources (wind, solar, geothermal, wave, tidal, hydropower, biomass, 
landfill gas, sewage treatment plant gas and biogases)” (no. 30, page 6). For the purpose of support fuels, the 
energy needs to be in a physical form that can be combusted to be used in the power plants, leaving the 
‘biomass’ and gaseous alternatives mentioned in the directive. The Directive 2009/28/EC (European Union, 
2009) further clarifies biomass as “'Biomass' means the biodegradable fraction of products, waste and residues 
from biological origin from agriculture (including vegetal and animal substances), forestry and related 
industries including fisheries and aquaculture, as well as the biodegradable fraction of industrial and municipal 
waste; it includes bioliquids and biofuels.” (article 2e, page 27). In this thesis there will be no distinction 
between bioliquids and biofuels, but the term biofuel will be used to cover all solid, liquid, or gaseous fuels 
produced from biomass.  
The biomass definition encompasses both the primary biofuels, which are biological material used directly in its 
unprocessed form (wood chips and -pellets, straw etc.) as well as Secondary biofuels, which is biomass 
processed into various types of fuels, as seen in figure C.1. Secondary biofuels can be produced from many 
different types of feedstock and using different technologies, and are therefore further divided into first- second- 
and third-generation biofuels based on these two parameters (Singh et al., 2011). It is important to note that the 
chemical and physical characteristics of the biofuel itself do not change between generations, but rather the 
feedstock it is derived from, changes. 
 
Figure C.1: adapted from Nigam and Singh, 2011 
First generation biofuels are produced from feedstock traditionally used as food, which are rich in starch, sugar, 
animal fats, and vegetable oils. The feedstock can be used for bioethanol production using yeast-derived 
enzymes to ferment the sugar. The oils can be used in biodiesel production by the process of transesterification 
(Nigam and Singh, 2011). The first generation biofuels are criticized for competing directly with food supply, 
==PAGE:109==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix C 
 
3 
 
thereby raising the cost of some crops and food stuffs, which is especially a problem in developing economies. 
Further only a small fraction of the total plant biomass (the seeds) is utilized in the process, reducing the land use 
efficiency (Nigam and Singh, 2011).  
Second generation biofuels primarily differ from first generation by being produced from non-edible crops or 
parts of crops. This can be from energy crops grown directly for the purpose, different types of wastes and 
residues from crops when they have fulfilled their main purpose. The difference in feedstock, usually 
lignocelluloses, also necessitates different technologies to extract energy from it. Fundamentally two approaches 
are used; biological (for alcohols) or thermo-chemical processing, which involve more extreme temperatures and 
pressures than processes used for first generation biofuels (Singh et al. 2011). The second generation biofuels 
limit the fuel vs food trade-off; however they still compete for the land and might cause direct or indirect land 
use change (LUC/iLUC), especially when energy crops are used. Due to the sophisticated production equipment 
larger scale facilities and higher investments are needed to produce second generation biofuels. It has therefore 
proved difficult to commercialise, and further research and development are needed in the area (Nigam and 
Singh, 2011 & Sims et al. 2010).  
Third generation biofuels are a classification category emanating from the second generation biofuels, referring 
specifically to biofuels derived from algae and microbial feedstock (Singh et al. 2011). The focus has been 
especially on microalgae for biofuel production because of a lot of beneficial characteristics. Algae can grow in a 
wide range of environmental conditions and represent a large diversity, which can be further enhanced and 
valuable characteristics can be improved by genetic modification. The diversity of the microalgae entails that it 
can be used for a large variety of biofuels, because they produce lipids, proteins, and carbohydrates I large 
amounts. Like all other photosynthetic plants algae take up CO2 and due to their rapid growth they can take up 
large amounts of both CO2 and nutrients like Nitrogen and Phosphorus in short periods of time, and increase 
yield significantly compared to any other crop. The major drawback of algae feedstock is that to reach high 
growth rates large amounts of water and nutrients are needed for the algal growth, even when grown in waste 
water, which could lead to a net emission of GHG’s due to fertilizer production (Biofuel.org., 2010). Also the 
cost of algae-based biofuels is much higher than of first and second generation biofuels, and the technology is 
still far from commercialisation (Scott, 2010).  
 
 
==PAGE:110==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix C 
 
4 
 
C.2. Long list of support fuels 
A literature review (Appendix E) was conducted to identify the possible options for bio-based SFs and create a 
long list of options as shown in Fejl! Henvisningskilde ikke fundet.. Each type of biofuel can be produced 
from various types of feedstock and in Fejl! Henvisningskilde ikke fundet. examples of feedstock in each 
generation are given, as well as a further classification related to the production processes. Generally the 
feedstock is either oil based or starch/cellulose based, and the processing is either biological (i.e. using 
microorganisms) or thermos-chemical. The only exception is Algae feedstock, which can theoretically be used 
for any type of biofuel since they contain both lipids and starch (Singh, 2010).  
The long list of biofuels is literature based, and therefore not all of the biofuels listed can actually be used at 
DONG Energy power plants. In appendix E a list of criteria is given, based on which the most promising of the 
biofuels are identified.

D.1. List of criteria 
To determine which of the biofuels mentioned in the long list (Appendix C) are the most promising as SFs a list 
of criteria was developed in collaboration with employees from DONG Energy. Even though DONG Energy has 
a lot of experience using biofuels (such as wood and straw) as their primary fuels, and even made experiments 
with burning rape seed oil at HEV (Herning power plant), there has been no previous work with bio-based SFs. 
The criteria for the SFs are therefore primarily based on requirements for the current, fossil SFs, and on general 
policies and criteria for fuels and procurement in DONG Energy. The lack of exact experimental data on SF-use 
entails that most of the criteria are not clear-cut, but rather guidelines for which parameters are important.  
Based on interviews and talks with DONG Energy employees three overall categories of criteria were identified; 
technical, market-related, and sustainability-related. The relevant criteria under each category are listed in 
Concluding limitations 
Fuels based on Danish straw cannot be used.  
D.2. Short list of alternative SFs 
When applying the above mentioned criteria to the long-list of alternative SFs the short-list shown in table D3 
remains. AS illustrated in Table D3 this results in eight different combinations of feedstock and processing 
method (i.e. type of fuel). . Due to the different nature of the criteria some of them relate to the fuel itself (e.g. 
the technical criteria), whereas other criteria relate to the feedstock or technology used to produce the biofuel 
(e.g. the sustainability-related criteria). This affiliation is also listed for each of the criteria in table D1. This 
study considers the use of SFs in DONG Energy specifically, however in order to use the results in more general 
contexts the possibility for generalisation of each criterion is noted in the table as well.

D.1.1. CSR and sustainability 
CSR and sustainability is a quite wide criteria category including both social and environmental considerations. 
The criteria in this category are primarily based on the DONG Energy Code of Conduct (DONG Energy, 2015) 
and the DONG Energy Programme for Sustainable Biomass Sourcing, DSPB (DONG Energy, 2014). The code 
of conduct builds on the UN guiding principles (United Nations, 2011), and includes principles on anti-
corruption, human rights, and labour rights.  Since these principles are followed by most large companies and 
include basic human rights this criterion is considered applicable for all energy producing companies. However, 
whether the criterion is followed depend on the specific management practices in the specific supply chain from 
which a fuel is procured. This principle is thus one the company should abide by when looking into buying fuels 
and not one that can be used to rule out a specific technology or feedstock from the study.  
The DPSB builds on the international Sustainable Biomass Partnership, SBP, which is an initiative, started in 
2013 by seven major European energy companies using wood-based biomass in large thermal power plants, who 
seeks to formulate common sustainability demands in the area (SBP, 2014). The SBP is to a large extent based 
on wood biomass, and the deforestation criterion comes from this. As with the code of conduct / Labour rights 
criterion this one is specific to the management practice and supply chain, and therefore should be used in a 
procurement situation rather than as reason for sorting out a feedstock or technology from this study.  
The criterion that the fuel cannot be first generation relates to the feedstock and the general consensus that first 
generation biofuels are not sustainable due to the direct competition between food and fuel that they offset 
(European Federation for Transport and Environment, 2015). First generation biofuels are here considered to be 
all fuels made from feedstock that could have been used for human food such as maize, sugar or eatable 
vegetable oils.  This relates to the criteria that the fuel cannot have obvious better uses. Just as feedstock suitable 
as food should not be used, neither should high-grade fuels that are essentially ”too good” to be used power 
plants, if these are limited and have better uses in e.g. the transport sector. In practice this criterion rules out 
drop-in fuel for the transport sector. These fuels include renewable diesel and biogasoline, which have chemical 
structures almost identical to that of fossil diesel and gasoline used for car engines, and hence can be used 
directly with current motor technologies without being blended into fossil fuels. Thus the drop-in fuels are 
developed specifically and refined to a degree where they can replace fossil diesel and gasoline in the transport 
sector 1:1. The renewable diesel and biogasoline are thus of too high quality to use at power plants.  
Concluding limitations 
None of the first generation feedstock types can be used.  
Neither biogasoline nor green diesel can be used.    
D.1.2. Compatibility with the existing system 
An important criterion for DONG Energy and other profit-driven companies if converting to bio-based SFs is to 
have a low capital expenditure, capex, thus the existing support fuel system at the plants should be able to handle 
the new SF with no or only minor changes. As described in appendix B, eight boilers at five plants use support 
fuels in DONG Energy, which is either HFO or natural gas as is the case for most dust fired power plants 
(DECC, 2014). For the system to be able to handle an alternative biofuel it should be in the same physical form, 
==PAGE:115==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix D 
 
3 
 
which means that natural gas can be replaced only by a gaseous alternative and heavy fuel oil by another oil-
based liquid.  
The gaseous alternatives include methane produced by microorganisms by degradation of organic material, 
which is grouped into the three production pathways: Biogas from biogas plants, Landfill gas collected at 
landfills, and gas collected at sewage treatment plants. In each case the gas is primarily a mixture of methane and 
carbon dioxide, of which the methane is the combustible part. However, the compatibility criterion entails no 
major changes in the storage facilities as well, and since the natural gas is delivered directly via the natural gas 
grid, there are no storage facilities for the gas. This means that the three types of gas should be upgraded to the 
natural gas standard, in order to be delivered through the natural gas grid to be used at the DONG Energy plants. 
The upgrading is basically a removal of the CO2 in the gas, which leads to increased methane concentration and 
thereby increased heating value.  
The plants currently using HFO as SF has a feeding mechanism adapted to this high viscosity oil fraction, which 
among other things, means that the system includes heating of the HFO to keep it fluid. This heating mechanism 
can, however, be adjusted or turned off if it is not needed. According to the technical experts in DONG Energy 
the HFO feeding system should be applicable for all types of oil-based biofuels as well as oil-based fossil fuels, 
as long as the chemical composition does not corrode the pipes, since the pumps and heating function should be 
easily adjusted to fit other oil types as well. However, the alcohol biofuels, such as bioethanol, cannot be used 
with the current system due to the very different properties, especially the low vaporization point. Also the 
current oil-tanks would be applicable for storing bio-based oil fuels (as long as it is not corrosive to the tanks), 
whereas the alcohols are too volatile and need a different type of storage, which would imply major changes of 
the storage system.  
Concluding limitations 
Only upgraded types of bio-based gases, which can be delivered via the natural gas grid, can be used as 
alternatives for natural gas. It depends on the market, whether the gas is upgraded, and how much is available.   
Only oil-based biofuels, and not alcohol-based biofuels, can be used as alternatives for HFO.  
D.1.3. Physical properties 
The reason for using SF’s is to ignite the primary fuel and to be able to run in the low-load zone, which cannot 
be done with the primary fuels due partly to their high flash points and other physical properties, as described in 
appendix B. Hence for a fuel to be useful as a SF it should be easy to ignite and maintain the flame, i.e. the 
ignition security should be high, since it entails high costs if flaws arise in the start-up of a boiler. The ignition 
security is dependent on the flash point (the temperature at which the fuel flashes when brought into contact with 
an ignition source) and the heating value (I.e. the amount of energy in the fuel), which are also important factors 
for how well the SF can ignite the primary fuel. Also the ability of the fuel to atomise into small enough droplets 
when fed into the boiler is important, an ability that is to some extent dependent on the viscosity of the fuel. 
Further the viscosity influences how well the fuel can move within the pipes if the feeding mechanism.  
No study has been made internally in DONG Energy of which values of flash point and heating values are 
sufficient to maintain a satisfying ignition security.  Therefore the properties of the current SFs, HFO and 
Natural gas, are used as guidelines for which interval these properties should lie in.  The values of both the 
current, fossil SFs and the bio-based alternatives are listed in table D2. 
==PAGE:116==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix D

D.1.4. Chemical composition 
The chemical composition of any fuel fed into the boilers is important for the plant maintenance and lifetime, 
since some chemical form substances that might cause slagging and fouling of the boiler or corrosion and tear. It 
is known from tests with alternative primary fuels that especially high amounts of chlorine and potassium should 
be avoided. Also too high nitrogen content and heavy metals should be avoided, since they cause emissions of 
NOx and heavy metals in the smoke and ash. However, there is a big difference from primary to support fuels, 
since the amount of support fuels that is used in the boilers is only between 1-5% of the total fuel consumption. 
The Heavy fuel oil used in most of the plants today is one of the most “polluted” and corroding fuels that can be 
fed to the boiler. Hence it is not possible beforehand to set any specific chemical limits for the fuels. However, 
the content of critical compounds in heavy fuel oil and natural gas is shown in table Y, which gives an indication 
of at least these compounds’ allowable limit. However, a chemical analysis should be made for any alternative 
support fuel before testing.  
D.1.5. Market-related criteria 
An important criterion for DONG Energy to consider alternative bio-based SFs is that it is economically 
feasible. Since most of the technologies and fuels considered (excluding the first generation biofuels) are still 
under development and some of them are not currently marketed, it is not possible to state a market price. 
Therefore the economic feasibility is considered in a long-term perspective based on technology development 
and current knowledge. Hence to be able to consider emerging technologies, both 2nd and 3rd generation biofuels 
are considered in this study. The technology development and market availability is strongly correlated to the 
price of the biofuels, and can also change considerably over time. It is therefore not possible to directly sort out 
any of the biofuel options, since the price development is not possible to predict. Rather this criterion should be 
considered as the market develops, and not be used to rule out any technical feasible option because the business 
case is not favourable at this moment. One way this criterion can be used, however, is to choose between 
==PAGE:117==
Environmental sustainability of support fuels in central power plants 
Master thesis at DTU – Appendix D 
 
5 
 
multiple similar options if the market for one is more developed than for others. This is for instance the case for 
biogas in Denmark. A report from Energistyrelsen (2013) shows that the majority of biogas in Denmark is 
produced from manure at farm- and joint biogas plants (4 PJ), and only very little at sewage plants and landfills. 
Further the manure based biogas is the only type of biogas plants connected to upgrading facilities, which makes 
it possible to deliver the gas directly via the natural gas grid. 
Since many of the bio-based fuel markets are small compared to those of fossil fuels and they are not traded as 
commodities it is also important for DONG Energy to compete internally for the same fuels, and hence feedstock 
based on biomass already used by DONG Energy is not preferred. This is for instance true for Danish straw-
based biofuels, such as pyrolysis oil based on wood or Danish straw.  
Concluding limitations 
Fuels based on Danish straw cannot be used.  
D.2. Short list of alternative SFs 
When applying the above mentioned criteria to the long-list of alternative SFs the short-list shown in table D3 
remains. AS illustrated in Table D3 this results in eight different combinations of feedstock and processing 
method (i.e. type of fuel).

Based on the reports of about 130 scientists

their blends with diesel. A vast majority of the 
scientists reported that short-term engine 
tests using vegetable oils as fuels were very 
promising but the long-term test results 
showed higher carbon built up and lubricating 
oil contamination, resulting in engine failure.   
The engine power output was found to be 
equivalent to that of diesel fuel. In addition, it 
observed that the base catalysts are more 
effective than acid catalysts and enzymes.

Fuel 89

Buyukkaya,  
Experimental tests were investigated to 
evaluate the performance, emission and 
combustion of a diesel engine using neat 
rapeseed oil and its blends of 5%, 20% and 
70%, and standard diesel fuel separately. 
The results indicate that the use of biodiesel 
produces lower smoke opacity (up to 60%). 
CO emissions of B5 and B100 fuels were 
found to be 9% and 32% lower than that of 
the diesel fuel. Combustion characteristics of 
rapeseed oil and its diesel blends closely 
followed those of standard diesel.

Anvendelse af 
biogas til el- og 
varmeproduktion 
-  analyser for 
biogas taskforce  
Energistyrels
en, 2014 
EA 
Energianalys
e 
økonomi og indpasningsmuligheder for 
biogas til el- og varme på både mellemlang 
og lang sigt. I analyserne værdisættes 
biogassen i konkurrence med en række 
alternative muligheder for energiforsyning. 
Vurdering af det samlede biomasse-
potentiale. Værdien af biogas til el- og 
varmeproduktion, er helt afhængig af hvilke 
alternative brændsler der er til rådighed. 
Analyserne viser, at det er samfunds-
økonomisk billigst at anvende biogas direkte 
(uden opgradering) de næste 10-20 år. 
Analyserne i denne rapport har vist, at 
omkostningerne ved at producere biogas er 
betydelige, og er højere end den værdi 
biogassen har ved produktion af el- og 
varme.

 The structure of the zeolites, in terms of pore 
size and Si/Al ratio (unit cell size), has been 
found to inﬂuence the catalytic cracking 
reactions and thus to affect the conversion 
level and the product distribution. On the 
basis of these ﬁndings, it would be of high 
interest to tune the zeolite structural 
parameters in order (i) to increase the 
methoxyphenol conversion and (ii) to 
decrease coke deposition from oxygenates.

more oil from algae than lipid extraction (LE) 
does because protein and carbohydrates are 
converted. Modelling all essential operations 
from nutrient manufacturing through fuel use.

nitrogen incorporated in the HTL oil was 
treated as lost. Consideration of nitrogen 
recycling during upgrading.

 To assess the tradeoffs of increased up-front 
emissions and reduced operational 
emissions, we present, to our knowledge, the 
first global, integrated life cycle assessment 
(LCA) of long-term, wide-scale 
implementation of electricity generation from 
renewable sources (i.e., photovoltaic and 
solar thermal, wind, and hydropower) and of 
carbon dioxide capture and storage for fossil 
power generation. Under the Baseline 
scenario, emissions of air and water 
pollutants more than double whereas the low-
carbon technologies introduced in the BLUE 
Map scenario allow a doubling of electricity 
supply while stabilizing or even reducing

This article evaluates the environmental 
performance of a biofuel production system 
based on the fast pyrolysis of short-rotation 
poplar biomass using a Life Cycle 
Assessment (LCA) approach. The system 
includes a circulating ﬂuidized bed reactor 
followed by bio-oil upgrading through 
hydrotreating and hydrocracking to produce 
gasoline and diesel.

 this study aims to compare and validate the 
production of biodiesel from palm and 
jatropha using the life cycle assessment 
(LCA) approach. The assessment 
encompasses the cultivation of the crop, the 
oil extraction stage and ﬁnally, the biodiesel 
production stage. We found that to produce 1 
tonne of jatropha biodiesel, the land area 
requirement is 118% higher than to produce 
1 tonne of palm biodiesel. The energy output-
to-input ratio for palm biodiesel is 2.27, 
slightly higher than jatropha biodiesel at 1.92. 
Furthermore, CO2  sequestration for the 
whole life cycle chain of palm biodiesel is 20 
times higher than jatropha biodiesel. All these 
results show the superiority and sustainability 
of palm oil as a feedstock for biodiesel 
production.

POME is a highly polluting wastewater with 
high chemical oxygen demand (COD) and 
biochemical oxygen demand (BOD). POME 
was identiﬁed as a potential source to 
generate renewable bioenergies such as 
biomethane and biohydrogen through 
anaerobic digestion. This review aims to 
discuss various technologies to convert 
POME to biomethane and biohydrogen in a 
commercial scale. Furthermore, discussion 
on using POME to culture microalgae for 
biodiesel and bioethanol production was 
included in the present paper as a new 
remedy to utilize POME with a greater 
beneﬁcial return.

 performance of the different cropping 
systems. beneﬁts of corn stover removal : 
Lower N from soil, higher ethnaol production 
/ha, energy recovery from lignin-rich 
fermentation residues. Cons: lower 
accumulation rate of soil organic carbon and 
higher fuel consumption in harvesting. Thus, 
utilization of corn stover and winter cover 
crops can improve the eco-efﬁciency of the 
cropping systems. Planting winter cover 
crops can compensate for some 
disadvantages (i.e., soil organic carbon levels 
and soil erosion) of removing corn stover.

has been undertaken to assess the energetic 
balance and the potential environmental 
impacts of the whole process chain, from the 
biomass production to the biodiesel 
combustion. conﬁrms the potential of 
microalgae as an energy source but 
highlights the imperative necessity of 
decreasing the energy and fertilizer 
consumption. It is a “from cradle to 
combustion” analysis for the fuel and a “from 
cradle to grave” analysis for the facility.

A two-step process consisting of pre-
esterification and transesterification was 
developed to produce biodiesel from crude 
Jatropha curcas L. oil. The yield of biodiesel 
by transesterification was higher than 98% in 
20 min using 1.3% KOH as catalyst and a 
molar ratio of methanol to oil 6:1 at 64 ◦C

 in this study a literature review on available 
and recently published life cycle assessment 
(LCA) studies for biodiesel has been carried 
out and the potential GHG emission savings 
from biodiesel production and use compared 
to fossil diesel have been analysed. underline 
the significant influence of the effects of land 
use change and the importance of avoiding 
the conversion of natural land into agricultural 
areas. In particular, the biodiesel feedstock 
production and the source of energy for the 
production process strongly influence the 
overall result of the GHG balance of 
biodiesel.

large number of life-cycle studies that have 
varying and sometimes contradictory 
conclusions. This article presentsa 
comprehensive review oflife-cycle studies 
ofbiodieselin Europe.  The main causes for 
this high variability have been investigated, 
with emphasis on modeling choices. Key 
issues found are treatment of co-product and 
land use modeling, including high uncertainty 
associated with N2O and carbon emissions 
from cultivated soil. Direct correlation 
between how soil emissions were modeled 
and increasing values for 
calculated GHG emission has been found.

 Dehydration of biomass-derived levulinic 
acid under solid acid catalysis and treatment 
of the resulting angelica lactone with catalytic 
K 2 CO 3 produces the angelica lactone 
dimer in excellent yield. This dimer serves as 
a novel feedstock for hydrodeoxygenation, 
which proceeds under relatively mild 
conditions with a combination of oxophilic 
metal and noble metal catalysts to yield 
branched C 7 –C 10 hydrocarbons in the 
gasoline volatility range. Considering that 
levulinic acid is available in > 80% 
conversion from raw biomass, a field-to-tank 
yield of drop-in, cellulosic gasoline of > 60% 
is possible. In no study that we are aware of 
have branched alkanes suitable for drop-in

 summarize seven grand challenges that 
must be confronted to enable life-cycle 
assessment (LCA) to e ﬀ ectively evaluate 
the environmental  “footprint ” of biofuel 
alternatives:                                                        
• understanding farmers, feedstock options, 
and land use 
• predicting biofuel production technologies 
and practices 
• characterizing tailpipe emissions and their 
health consequences 
• incorporating spatial heterogeneity in 
inventories and assessments 
• accounting for time in impact assessments 
• assessing transitions as well as end states 
• confronting uncertainty and variability

Using palm oil sludge (residue from all palm 
oil mills) as feedstock for biofuel, to make a 
substitute for fuel oil (oil no 4 and 6, which 
are residue fossil oils) used inheavy 
machineries.  Current production cost of any 
biofuels is much superior  that made residual 
oil to be a fuel of choice especially for heavy 
machineries. This research aims to reduce 
the production cost of biofuels by utilizing 
Sludge Palm Oil (SPO) as its feedstock.

The methane productivity of manure in terms 
of volatile solids (VS), volume and livestock 
production was determined.  higher in pig 
(356 l kg −1 VS) and sow manure (275 l kg 
−1 VS) than in dairy cattle manure (148 l kg 
−1 VS).  methane yield of straw was found to 
be higher than the yield 
from total manure and the solid fractions of 
manure, due to the higher VS content, and 
hence the use of straw as bedding material 
will increase the volumetric as well as the 
livestock-based methane productivity.

Biogas plants in 
Denmark: 
successes and 
setbacks 
Renewable 
and 
Sustainable 
Energy 
Reviews 
11 (2007) 
116–132 
R.P.J.M. 
Raven, K.H. 
Gregersen 
 This article reviews the experimental 
introduction of biogas plants in Denmark 
since the 1970s. We argue that three factors 
have been important for the current status of 
biogas plants in Denmark. First, the Danish 
government applied a bottom-up strategy and 
stimulated interaction and learning between 
various social groups. Second, a dedicated 
social network and a long-term stimulation 
enabled a continuous development of biogas 
plants without interruptions until the late 
1990s. Third, speciﬁc Danish circumstances 
have been beneﬁcial.  The current setback in 
biogas plants is mainly caused by a shift in 
energy and environmental policies and 
limited availability of organic waste.

A Framework for

J. H.

The link between demand for crops in  one  
region  and  impacts  in  other  regions  is  
referred  to  here  as  indirect  land  use  
change (iLUC) and includes deforestation, 
intensification and reduced consumption. 
Existing models for  iLUC  tend  to  ignore  
intensification  and  reduced  consumption. 
This paper presents the conceptual 
framework required for a consistent 
modelling of iLUC in Life Cycle Assessment.

A few properties, in particular the extremely 
high viscosity and high water content, 
complicate the use of straight castor oil as a 
fuel for internal combustion engines. A better 
perspective may be possible by 
transesterification and the addition of this 
biodiesel to fossil diesel fuel. This, however, 
calls for considerably lower castor oil prices 
than are at present being paid on the world 
market. question is whether castor oil is 
suitable as a fuel and whether it has a 
chance on national and international markets 
for other purposes.

free fatty acid profile of jatropha curcas oil 
has been determined and 
compared with literature studies. It is found 
that jatropha curcas mainly contains 13.0% 
palmitic acid, 44.5% oleic acid and 35.4% 
linoeic acid. Moreover, jatropha curcas 
biodiesel was produced using different 
acid catalysts (HCL and H2SO4) and alkaline 
catalysts (NaOH, KOH, CH3ONa and 
CH3OK). It has been reported that 
CJCO has high free fatty acids content (41%) 
and consequently fail to convert directly to 
biodiesel using alkaline catalyzed 
transesteriﬁcation process.

Although significant progress continues to be 
made to overcome the technical and 
economic challenges, 2nd-generation 
biofuels production will continue to face major 
constraints to full commercial deployment. 
The objectives are to examine the current 
state of technological development with 
emphasis on bio-ethanol, evaluate and 
compare the production costs, outline the 
policies necessary to best overcome 
constraints and support development and 
deployment; and determine the future 
challenges to be reached if full 
commercialisation is to occur.

three dimensions, i.e. social, environmental,

The life cycle assessment (LCA) of algal 
biofuels suggests them to be environmentally 
better than the fossil fuels but economically it 
is not yet so attractive. Significant 
improvements in the efficiency, cost structure 
and ability to scale up algal growth, lipid 
extraction, and biofuel production must be 
made to produce commercially viable biofuel. 
At 
the current stage of development, it is still too 
early to comments on any preferred routes of 
biofuels production from algal biomass. 
Comprehensive life cycle assessment of 
algal biofuels illustrating 
environmental benefits and impacts can and 
should be a tool for guiding technology 
development as well as for policy decisions.

APPLIED

D. Tonini, T:

Environmental impacts related to potential 
future energy systems in Denmark with high 
shares of wind and biomass energy were 
evaluated using life-cycle assessment (LCA). 
Three alternatives for supply of transport 
fuels were considered: (1) fossil fuels, (2) 
rapeseed based biodiesel, and (3) Fischer– 
Tropsch based biodiesel. showed that 
greenhouse gas emissions per PJ energy 
supplied could be significantly reduced (from 
68 to 17 Gg CO2-eq/PJ) by increased use of 
wind and residual biomass. Energy crops for 
production of biofuels and the use of these 
biofuels for heavy terrestrial transportation 
were responsible for most environmental 
impacts; land use changes (LUCs), fertilizer 
use and NOx emissions. For global warming, 
biodiesel production via Fischer–Tropsch 
was comparable with fossil diesel. 
 
land occupation was included 
in the assessment according to the IMPACT

It is difficult to draw general conclusions from 
the set of studies due to the variation in 
outcomes. Causes of this variation include 
real-world differences, data uncertainties and 
methodological choices. In this review we 
explore some of the more complicated 
sources of differences in findings related to 
LCA methodology by reviewing 67 LCA 
studies published between 2005 and 2010. 
Specifcially difficult to solve coproduct 
allocation. The treatment of biogenic carbon 
is another important issue. Most studies 
include neither extractions nor emissions of 
biogenic CO2, but a number of these LCAs 
do include both, leading to very different 
conclusions on greenhouse gas performance 
of biofuel chains.

waste generation constitutes one of the major 
environmental problems where production 
industries are concerned.. palm oil industry is 
one of the leading industries in Malaysia 
covering 11% of the Malaysian land area. 
However, the production of such amounts of 
crude palm oil result in even larger amounts 
of palm oil mill efﬂuent (POME), 
estimated at nearly three times the quantity 
of crude palm oil. Normally, POME is treated 
using end-of-pipe processes, but it is worth 
considering the potential value of POME. The 
present paper thus discusses various 
technically feasible and economically 
beneﬁcial means of transforming the POME 
into low or preferably high value added 
products

==PAGE:167==
MASTER THESIS IN ENVIRONMENTAL TECHNOLOGY AT DTU ENVIRONMENT 
Appendix G 
Fuel consumption during Start-up 
 
Mete Rames 
02-06-2015 
 
 
 
 
 
In this appendix the fuel consumption, both primary fuel and support fuel, during a shut-down and start-up of the different boilers are presented. The 
share of produced energy from the support fuels are calculated based on the input-energy percentage. The plants SSV, ESV3, ASV2, ASV5, and AVV1 all 
use HFO as support fuel, and are presented first. The plants HEV1 and AVV2 both use natural gas and are presented in the last two tables.
